*Immortal-Transmitted Secret External Medicine Formulas*

Compiled by Yang Qingsou of the Yuan dynasty; edited by Zhao Yizhen of the Ming dynasty.

*Source Text:* *Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era*, *Taiping* Section.

---

**Preface to *Immortal-Transmitted Verified External Medicine Formulas***

In my youth, while studying, I often heard the ancients say: "As a child, one must not be ignorant of medicine." Thus, whenever I encountered fine medical texts, I delighted in transcribing them, accumulating dozens of volumes. When I saw the sick, it was as if I myself were afflicted, and I frequently prepared medicines to distribute.

One day, my late father admonished me: "Giving medicine to others is not as good as giving them formulas, for then the aid you provide will be far-reaching." Thereafter, whenever I obtained a verified formula, I shared it with those of benevolent intent.

Later, I retreated into reclusion beyond the worldly realm, abandoning my former studies. Moreover, after enduring the dust and calamities of countless kalpas, all was reduced to ashes, leaving nothing behind. Only the *Collection of Verified External Medicine Formulas*—compiled by Yang Qingsou of Hechuan and transmitted to Wu Ningji, whose son Youzhang passed it to Master Li Shanguan of Xiping, who in turn entrusted it to me—remained. Its formulas are concise and essential, yet regrettably, it has never been printed. Thus, I preserved it alone.

Recently, while traveling through the sacred land of Jinjing, I passed through Yudu. My disciple Xiao Tianni of Fenggang, a scion of a prominent family from Xichang, had studied the Dao at Ziyang Abbey for twenty years. He had previously accompanied me in my travels and was capable of summoning wind and rain to relieve droughts and floods—a testament to his deep karmic affinity with the Dao and his steadfast practice.

His fellow disciple, Liu Zhirou of Shunchuan, had suffered for years from sores and boils covering his body. He had taken *Jing Huang Tang*, *Bai Du San*, and other medicines, all to no avail. I therefore entrusted him with an external medicine formula, and after taking *Fan Hun Tang* for less than a full course, he was cured. Delighted, Tianni donated his own funds to have the text block-printed and distributed, so that its beneficent intent might circulate widely. In this, his response to prayers for rain or sunshine was indeed fitting.

Nevertheless, I recall an old friend who suffered from *guzhui* wind, enduring alternating chills and fever for months, bedridden and beyond the help of many physicians. Finally, a doctor examined him and said: "Though this is a chronic ailment, there remains an exogenous pathogen in the Shaoyang channel that has not been resolved. If he has taken *Wu Ji San*, it would have been a mistake." Upon inquiry, this proved to be the case. The doctor then administered several doses of *Xiao Chai Hu Tang*, which promptly dispelled the chills and fever. He subsequently used this specialty’s *Zhui Feng Wan* and other medicines to address the wind disorder, leading to a full recovery.

Miscellaneous illnesses have their formulas, and febrile disorders have their methods. Only when a physician masters both can they be deemed truly skilled. If one specializes solely in major formulas or external medicine, there is a risk of unknowingly making errors, which would hardly be perfect.

Though the discussions of syndromes and prescriptions in this external medicine text are profoundly reasoned and superior to other formulas, those of discerning judgment must still exercise careful consideration when applying them in practice.

Written on the first day of the ninth month of the *wuwu* year of the Hongwu era,
by Zhao Yizhen, Yuanyangzi of Junyi.

---

Medicine has long been esteemed as an art. Since the Divine Farmer Shennong first tasted the hundred herbs, distinguishing their warm, cool, cold, and hot natures to save the people from premature death, his merit has been unparalleled. Later, Bian Que and Hua Tuo mastered its techniques most exquisitely, their treatments and cures verging on the divine. Yet their arts were not fully transmitted. Today’s external medicine is but a remnant of their legacy.

Among external disorders, carbuncles and abscesses are the most severe. Abscesses differ in their yin-yang and deficiency-excess patterns; a slight error in medication can immediately lead to danger. Other conditions, such as nail-like sores and throat afflictions, are even more lethal. Though there are no shortage of external medicine practitioners in the world, few achieve true mastery.

The alchemist Zhao Yuanyang of Junyi, renowned for his erudition, inherited the teachings of the Laozi school. His Daoist practice was lofty and pure, surpassing his peers, and his heart was deeply committed to aiding others. He compiled rare and extraordinary medical texts into volumes, but most were lost in the chaos of war and fire. Only the external medicine formulas survived.

In the autumn of the *wuwu* year, he traveled with his book to Jinjing, lodging at Ziyang Abbey in Yudu—a place where, twenty years earlier, he had transmitted Daoist methods to the abbey’s adept, Xiao Fenggang. Upon his return, he cured Fenggang’s disciple Liu Shunchuan of a chronic, untreatable skin ailment. Fenggang then resolved to have the text printed to broaden its life-saving intent. However, Yudu’s remote valleys made it difficult to find craftsmen, and the project was delayed until the fifth month of the *rentu* year, when Yuanyang ascended as an immortal. Before his passing, he entrusted his disciples with the task of fulfilling his wish, compiling the secret formulas he had received into a single volume.

Yuanyang’s own preface, along with Fenggang’s donation for printing, reveals their utmost sincerity and benevolence. The circulation of this book will rescue those in peril and aid in emergencies, ensuring that all who are children may possess it and avoid the sin of filial impiety, and that all who are physicians may possess it and avoid the failing of heartlessness. Herein lies the heart of the benevolent and the filial.

Thus, I write this preface, that the diligence of its compilation may not be in vain.

Written in the *yangfu* month of the Hongwu era,
by Wu Youren, Youlanfu of Luling.

---

**Immortal-Transmitted Secret External Medicine Formulas**
*Volume One*
Compiled by Zhao Yizhen, Yuanyangzi of Junyi.

**Preface and Discourse on Abscesses, Carbuncles, and Dorsal Boils**
*Section One*

In external medicine, abscesses and carbuncles are placed before all other ailments because their transformations occur in an instant, and life hangs by a thread.

Though abscesses and carbuncles bear over twenty names, their essence reduces to two: the *yin* and *yang* patterns. Those arising from *yang* are called *yong* (abscesses), characterized by heat and excess; those from *yin* are called *ju* (carbuncles), marked by cold and deficiency.

When *yang* erupts, the skin is thin, the color red, the swelling elevated, and often dotted with dozens of pinhole-like openings that cause pain. When *yin* erupts, the skin is thick, the color pale, the swelling hard—like the hide of an ox’s neck—and painless.

There are also *yin* within *yang*: seeming heat that is not true heat, swelling that is outwardly firm but inwardly deficient, redness without dryness, a desire to ache yet no pus, swelling that rises then subsides, and external fullness with internal flesh decay. And *yang* within *yin*: seeming cold that is not true cold, no swelling yet internal excess, faint redness with dryness, pus with pain, and though the exterior appears unremarkable, the interior is vexed and stifled.

Those with *yin* within *yang* tend to be plump, their flesh taut but their interiors deficient. Those with *yang* within *yin* tend to be lean, their flesh slack but their interiors replete.

Moreover, *yang* may transform into *yin* due to the misapplication of cooling herbs by inept physicians, while *yin* may shift to *yang* through the abrupt use of potent warming formulas. Yet when *yang* turns to *yin*, the condition is more common, and recovery—return to *yang*—remains possible, so survival is frequent. When *yin* turns to *yang*, the condition is rarer, and once lost, *yang* cannot be restored, so death is common. The rare survivors owe their lives to physicians who, by chance, apply the correct method—one in a hundred.

The term *eruption* refers to what accumulates within and manifests without. Generally, the entire human body is rooted in the five *zang*-organs, and the *qi* of the five *zang* all derive from the stomach’s *qi*. The stomach is the foundation of the five *zang*; it receives grain, and the spleen transforms it to generate *qi*. The spleen governs flesh and muscle, while the stomach’s *qi* transmits to the five *zang*, propelling blood and vessels to circulate through the channels and collaterals, completing a full cycle day and night.

Though abscesses and carbuncles vary in deficiency, excess, cold, and heat, all arise from *qi* stagnation. Their causes are three: internal, external, and neither internal nor external.

Internal causes are assessed at *renying* (the inch pulse on the left wrist, one *fen* before the bar). External causes are assessed at *qikou* (the inch pulse on the right wrist, one *fen* before the bar). If the pulses at *renying* and *qikou* are harmonious, the cause is neither internal nor external.

Their origins are five:
1. Epidemic seasonal *qi*,
2. Internal stagnation of the seven emotions,
3. Constitutional deficiency with external contraction,
4. Body heat contending with wind-cold,
5. Consumption of roasted meats, strong wine, or alchemical minerals—all sources of heat toxin.

From these five, pathogenic *qi* stagnates in the stomach. If the stomach’s *qi* is robust and the body replete, the pathogen contends with it, flowing into the channels and collaterals, obstructing where blood and vessels are sluggish, converging and congealing into abscesses. If the stomach’s *qi* is weak and the body deficient, the pathogen prevails, lodging in the channels and collaterals, coagulating and accumulating, causing blood and vessels to stagnate, leading to internal decay and carbuncles.

Thus it is said:
- Outwardly like a millet grain, yet within it may hold a measure of grain;
- Outwardly like a coin, yet within it may cradle a fist;
- Vile toxin and pus-filled tracts, an inch long and deeply replete;
- Pus and blood both withered—treatable with medicine;
- Foul rot without strands, blood decayed and *qi* depleted;
- Intestines severed, *yin* exuberant—even immortals cannot heal.

In treatment, the physician must gauge the patient’s deficiency or excess, discern the cold or heat of the illness, trace its cause, investigate its origin, and only then administer care. Internal and external must correspond; one cannot generalize.

If the illness erupts from *yang* with extreme heat, one should first regulate the *qi* and harmonize the blood. When *qi* is regulated, toxic *qi* disperses and does not stagnate; when blood is harmonized, vessels flow and the swelling naturally scatters. For *qi* is *yang*, blood is *yin*; when *yin* and *yang* are balanced, the patient is at ease.

Externally, apply cooling medicines to stimulate it. If heat is severe, blood cools and scatters more readily. If it does not scatter, the heat will have abated, yet blood will congeal from the cold—this is the gradual shift from *yang* to *yin*, the root of putrefaction. One must swiftly return to cooling treatment, resolving the external attack on the surrounding blood pathways, draining the already-formed pus and toxin within, and then proceed according to method to achieve success.

If the illness erupts from *yin* with extreme cold, internally use balanced tonics to diffuse the *qi*, nourish the blood, and support the original *yang*, following the spleen and stomach. Wait until appetite returns and vitality revives, then regulate *qi* and harmonize blood as usual.

Externally, apply warming medicines to mobilize the *qi* and blood throughout the body, revive dead flesh, and draw out toxic *qi*, then use mild warming medicines to disperse it. In cases of extreme cold, or those misled by cold medicines, one may resort to the *Three Revivers* to restore *yang*. The illness will then erupt laterally and recur—this is auspicious, a sign of *yin* transforming into *yang* and renewed life. If internal *yang* does not return and external signs do not appear, this is solitary *yang* severed from *yin*—beyond remedy.

For *yang* is *qi*, and *yin* is blood. When *yang* moves, *yin* follows; when *qi* circulates, blood flows. If *qi* does not circulate, blood dies; if blood dies, flesh dies; if flesh dies, the illness is fatal.

In cold patterns, warming medicines are used simply to move *qi* and blood. For blood and *qi* move when warmed and halt when cooled.

Though the skillful use of cooling and warming medicines is essential, one must first administer frankincense and mung bean powder to rescue the heart and protect the membrane. The heart governs the entire body, and the membrane encases the five *zang*. At the onset of illness, toxin invariably attacks the pericardium. Thus, some first experience retching, then abscesses or carbuncles; others first develop abscesses or carbuncles, then retching—because the pericardium’s root lies in the heart.

If treatment is delayed, the *pericardium*—the heart’s sovereign—suffers from toxin, and the spirit has no refuge; the primordial *qi* then falls into darkness. At the onset of illness, toxin invariably corrodes the flesh at the periphery. If treatment is delayed, the toxic *qi* penetrates the membrane; once the membrane is breached, the primordial *qi* leaks away, the *zang-fu* lose their nourishment, the spirit withers, and the pulse collapses entirely.

Thus, those who survive even severe illness are those in whom the five *zang* remain visible and the membrane intact. Yet some die from seemingly minor afflictions—where the flesh has not yet ulcerated but the membrane has already been breached. This is why rescuing the heart and protecting the membrane is the foremost principle.

This formula was secretly transmitted by immortals, its divine and ingenious workings beyond full description—far beyond the reach of ordinary physicians who rely on mere herbs, needles, or blades. Those who grasp its essence must treasure it well.

**Formula #2: Rong-Wei Fan-Hun Decoction**
*(Also called Tong-Shun Powder or He Shou-Wu Powder)*

**Ingredients:**
- *He Shou-Wu* (processed without iron contact)
- *Dang Gui*
- *Mu Tong* (peeled, nodes removed)
- *Chi Shao-Yao* (stir-fried)
- *Bai Zhi* (unexposed to fire)
- *Hui Xiang* (stir-fried)
- *Tu Wu-Yao* (stir-fried)
- *Chen Zhi-Qiao* (stir-fried with wheat flour; if nausea occurs, stir-fry with ginger juice)
- *Gan Cao*

This formula contains only these nine ingredients, in equal parts. Prepare as a decoction with water and wine, adjusting the ratio according to the pattern. For *liu-zhu* (migratory abscesses), add *Du Huo*.

**Dosage:** Four *qian* per dose. For illnesses in the upper body, take after meals; for those in the lower body, take before meals.

This single remedy is indispensable for *liu-zhu*, *yong-ju* (deep-rooted abscesses), *fa-bei* (back carbuncles), and traumatic fractures. It alone can rescue moribund conditions, revive dead flesh, quell crises before they arise, and eradicate roots after recovery. Its sovereign, minister, assistant, and envoy ingredients interact like the four seasons and five phases, waxing and waning in succession—truly an immortal’s miraculous formula. With appropriate modifications, its efficacy is boundless.

Why? Because this medicine powerfully harmonizes *qi* and regulates blood. *Qi* is *yang*; blood is *yin*. When *yang* moves, *yin* follows; when *qi* circulates, blood flows. If *yang* stagnates, *yin* congeals; if *qi* weakens, blood dies. When blood dies, flesh dies; when flesh dies, the illness is invariably fatal. Thus, one must regulate *yang* and harmonize *yin*—neither can be neglected.

If only *yang* is regulated without harmonizing *yin*, *qi* dissipates and blood congeals, leaving the flesh lifeless—like the *Wu-Xiang Lian-Qiao* formulas. If only *yin* is harmonized without regulating *yang*, blood flourishes while *qi* weakens, and the illness will recur—like the *Nei-Bu Shi-Xuan* formulas. Yet these two approaches may also be used in combination; rigidity yields no miracles. This formula fortifies the stomach’s root, spares the primordial *qi*, and scours away pathogenic filth, naturally restoring smooth circulation without giving rise to complications—truly an immortal’s elixir.

**Applications and Modifications:**

1. **For long-standing *fa-bei* (back carbuncles) that fail to heal:**
   This is due to prior excessive use of cooling medicines, which internally injure the spleen and externally freeze the blood. The spleen governs flesh; when it is damaged, appetite wanes, complexion withers, and flesh fails to regenerate. Blood fills the vessels; when it is frozen, *qi* loses its vigor, and flesh putrefies. Thus, the spleen must be regulated—once it is strong, flesh will regenerate. In this formula, remove *Mu Tong*, reduce *Dang Gui*, and double the amounts of *Hou Po* and *Chen Pi*. For severe cases, use the family’s *Dui-Jin Yin-Zi*; for even graver cases, add *Bai Dou-Kou* and the like.

2. **For *liu-zhu* (migratory abscesses):**
   Add *Du Huo*. *Liu-zhu* arises from *qi* and blood stagnation; when *qi* flows and stagnates, blood pools and congeals. This herb can invigorate the entire body’s blood vessels—once the vessels move, how can *liu-zhu* persist?

3. **For *liu-zhu* arising from *shang-han* (cold damage):**
   If the exterior has not fully resolved, residual toxin flows into the four limbs’ channels and collaterals, lodging where it stagnates and forming *liu-zhu*.
   - If the illness still presents tidal fever, the interior retains unresolved cold evil. To this formula, add *Sheng Ma* and *Su Ye*.
   - If fever persists after taking this, add *Gan Ge*.
   - If headache is present, add *Chuan Xiong* and decoct with ginger water.
   - If no tidal fever is present, decoct with equal parts water and wine, as wine powerfully moves blood and generates *qi*. When *qi* is generated and blood flows, recovery is assured.

   Why must *liu-zhu* be treated with exterior-resolving methods? To trace the cause, investigate the source, and not forget the illness’s root. Once cold evil is fully expelled, over-releasing the exterior may lead to *cold liu-zhu*, which is even more difficult to treat. Thus, mild exterior resolution is optimal.

   After releasing the exterior, the second stage calls for warm, neutral medicines—such as *Shi-Xuan* or *Nei-Bu*. If ineffective, the third stage may require adding *Fu-Zi* or administering several doses of *Si-Zhu San*. However, warming medicines must not be overused, lest they exacerbate suffering and prevent pus and blood from drying.

   The fourth stage returns to the original formula to achieve resolution.

   If the exterior is not fully resolved, residual toxin may attach to the bone, forming *gu-yong* (bone abscess). *Liu-zhu* is the remnant toxin of *shang-han*; *gu-yong* is the deteriorated pattern of *liu-zhu*. *Liu-zhu* is not the fault of *shang-han*, but of the physician’s incomplete exterior resolution. *Gu-yong* is not the fault of *liu-zhu*, but of the quack’s excessive use of cooling medicines.

   Quacks, blind in mind and deaf in intent, falsely claim clairvoyance. Even when they recognize *gu-yong*, their treatments lack method. They recklessly administer cooling medicines and apply caustic blades, causing toxic *qi* to stagnate. Cooling medicines and metal instruments drive the toxin deeper into the bone, making it incurable.

   Otherwise, how could human bones develop abscesses? Once bone becomes abscessed, no medicine can cure it—hence the names *fu-gu-ju* (bone-attaching carbuncle) and *bai-hu-fei-shi* (white tiger flying corpse). It lingers for years, shifting and turning, until the cold toxin and decayed bone are fully expelled before healing occurs.

Those that do not heal may persist for a lifetime—all due to initial mismanagement. When the bone decays, if it is an accessory bone, recovery is still possible; but if it is a primary bone, the condition becomes a lifelong disability.

When the pus is white and thin, the fragmented bone has just begun to detach, and probing the flesh to retrieve it is difficult. When the pus is yellow and thick, the fragmented bone is about to emerge, and the flesh is shallow enough for retrieval—this should be done with a sharp blade. Details are provided in later chapters. This, however, is but a general outline of treating *gu-yong*.

There are also cases where the illness has persisted for months, injured by blades, leaving the patient emaciated, limbs contracted, coughing up pus and blood, with decayed flesh and yin putrefaction. This arises from extreme cold, yang weakness, and yin excess—it must not be mistaken for heat due to blood in the sputum. It should be treated with modified formulas containing high-quality *fuzi*.

Other cases begin with toxin arising in the hands, feet, head, or face, causing pain throughout the body, ascending to the neck. Where the meridians converge, the sores resemble strings of pearls—this is a condition of wind-dampness and *qi* stagnation. It should be treated with modified *Xiao Xuming Tang* and *Du Huo Jisheng Tang*, used in conjunction with this formula.

There are also cases where pain begins in both knees and spreads to all joints, resembling "blood wind" in women and "soft wind" in men—this is called *fengshi bi* (wind-damp impediment) or *li-jie* (pervasive node). It should be treated with modified *Fuzi Ba Wu Tang*.

When abscesses form in the neck, armpits, beside the breasts, or in the soft flesh of the hips, they are called *chenjing yong* (cold abscesses). These are cold conditions without heat and should be treated with *Nei Bu Shi Xuan San*, used in conjunction with this formula. For children, *fuzi* must not be used lightly, lest it provoke fright seizures. Under no circumstances should needles or blades be applied. With thin blood and no pus, the flesh is reluctant to knit—apply warming, damp-dispersing medicines to resolve it internally. If needles or blades are mistakenly used, causing proud flesh to form, this same medicine will still achieve results. Should mild pain or slight seizures occur during treatment, use modified *Quan Xie Guanyin San*. Once the fright subsides, resume the original prescription.

There are also children with long-standing phlegm misdirected by illness, manifesting abscesses on the neck, arms, chest, or back—this is extreme cold, and success depends entirely on the application of warming medicines. Leaving an opening is preferable, as the condition may require further intervention; treatment methods follow later.

There is also *liuzhu* as large as a gourd or an overturned bowl appearing on the chest or back. Its symptoms resemble *fa* (effusion) but are far more severe. After administering medicine, if the form and momentum show even the slightest movement, it is no longer *fa*. It should be treated with *Nei Bu Shi Xuan* in conjunction with this formula, adjusted according to the condition, to resolve it internally.

Generally, all such conditions originate from cold. Where there is pain, it is bone pain—bone being the surplus of the kidney. When the kidney is deficient, the bone becomes cold, and cold bone is the cause of pain. The term *gu-ju* (bone carbuncle) arises from the kidney because its root lies there. Thus, to tonify the kidney, only large doses of *fuzi* can be effective. When the kidney is replete, the bone regains its vitality, and carbuncles will not attach to the bone.

In administering medicine, one must not be rigid but adaptable. Generally, when *yong* (abscesses) and *ju* (carbuncles) first emerge, they form from *qi* and blood stagnation. Over time, blood accumulates at the site of stagnation, eventually erupting. In patients with abundant *qi* and blood, *danggui* should be reduced in this formula, for excess generates blood, which may cause abscesses to form elsewhere, leading to endless recurrence. This is a secret transmission, rarely known to physicians.

There are two types of phlegm associated with *yong* and *ju*:
1. Stomach cold generating phlegm—add *banxia* to this formula to fortify the spleen and transform phlegm.
2. Heat constraint forming wind-phlegm—add *jiegeng* to this formula to dissolve phlegm in the throat and diaphragm, and decoct with fresh ginger in a mixture of water and wine.

For *nao-fa* (brain effusion) or *bei-fa* (back effusion) occurring above, remove *mutong* from this formula, lest it drain the lower *yuan*, complicating treatment for conditions of upper excess and lower deficiency. The elderly and weak should especially avoid it.

If the patient has diarrhea, this formula must not be used immediately. First, administer an antidiarrheal: dissolve raw *baifan* into powder, mix with melted yellow wax to form pills, and take thirty with rice water. Only after the diarrhea ceases should this medicine be used. The human body relies on *qi* and blood as its foundation. In those with abscesses, *qi* and blood surge to one place to form pus. If the viscera are not secure, *yuan-qi* will leak, and the blood will grow colder, making recovery difficult. This formula strongly regulates *qi*.

Generally, when *qi* flows smoothly, blood circulates; when *qi* is depleted, blood turns cold; when *qi* is cold, blood dies; and when blood dies, flesh does not regenerate. Applying warming medicines may fail to generate flesh if *yuan-qi* is insufficient to sustain it, only adding to suffering. Applying cooling medicines is entirely misguided.

Though this formula was divinely transmitted, its efficacy depends on proper use—otherwise, one risks the folly of "marking the boat to find the sword." As for *liuzhu*, it cannot be treated uniformly. If cooling medicines deplete and scatter *yuan-qi*, some cases may respond to *San Jian*, for such illnesses often stem from cold. One must carefully examine the pulse and differentiate the condition, confirming its cold origin before using such medicines—yet even then, excess is harmful. When the yang pulse revives, the swollen area turns red and lively, and the bone regains vitality, cold *qi* will no longer attach to form carbuncles. At that point, credit the original formula for its success—this is the infallible method.

This medicine may be prepared as a decoction, powder, or pill, with water or wine as the vehicle, chosen as circumstances dictate. For the affluent, coat it with *muxiang*. If the patient has heat-phlegm and cough, the wealthy may take it with *chenxiang*, the poor with *suye* decoction. For pills, use honey as the binder.

This formula is not only for treating *yong*, *ju*, *fa-bei* (effusion of the back), and fractures—it is also effective for men and women with *shan-qi* (hernia) or blood *qi* conditions, with repeated success. There was a woman who suffered from a *qi* illness for five years. When it flared, she experienced only lump pain, retching, and an inability to keep down water or food. Each episode brought her near death. She took this medicine as pills with *muxiang* decoction...

A single dose stops the retching; a second dose harmonizes phlegm and qi, and she was cured.

All cases of fractures and injuries invariably rely on this formula, though adjustments in additions and subtractions vary—details are found in the section on fractures and injuries. For common bruises, breaks, or damage, including injury to the pericardium, it treats all. If the injury is on the head, remove *mutong* and *zhike*, and add *chuanxiong* and *chenpi*. Routinely adding *dingpi* and *suye* activates blood; adding *poguzhi* and *wulingshi* breaks up static blood. Boil the decoction thoroughly, then steep it in a cup of strong wine. When it boils again, add *dahuang* powder and take it on an empty stomach. If the bowels move smoothly, only four doses are needed: the first two include *dahuang* powder, while the latter two omit it, merely promoting descent. If the bowels do not move, use *zhike* decoction to stimulate them continuously. If still no movement occurs, the condition is untreatable. One must not sit idly by while a person dies without knowing why. Consider formulas like *Buxue Shixuan San* and others.

For all fractures and injuries, this formula may omit *mutong* and be called *Heshouwu San*, as *heshouwu* supports blood. For blade wounds with tidal fever, facial swelling, and panting—signs of *poshangfeng* (tetanus)—take *Suoxue San* and *Gegen Tang* for several doses, or decoct *jiang* and *cong* for dispersing, or take *Baidusan* three or four times. Externally, apply topical medicines according to method; none will fail to heal.

For chronic lumbar pain, add *caobixie* and *xuanhusuo*, and decoct with wine. For *jiaoqi* (leg qi), add *binglang*, *mugua*, and *chuanshanjia*, and decoct with water.

Phlegm that has lost its proper path is not only unrecognized by others but, since its transmission from the immortals, only my lineage has known of it. Phlegm lubricates the entire body like a fish’s slime. When phlegm resides in the stomach and remains still, there is no illness; when it stirs, a hundred diseases arise—wheezing, coughing, retching, dizziness, headaches, eye pain, generalized stiffness, and painful joints are all caused by newly accumulated phlegm, formed from the败浊 congealing of blood and qi. Why? When the qi of the viscera and bowels rebels and stagnates, phlegm is born; when sweat should be released but is not, phlegm accumulates; when food and drink are overconsumed, fluids fail to move and gather into phlegm. Normally, phlegm travels from the stomach cavity to the lung tract and exits. When it loses its path, it scatters from the stomach cavity into the cold flesh, muscles, and skin. The spleen governs muscles, and the lungs govern skin and hair, so phlegm may lodge in the chest, back, head, neck, armpits, hips, waist, legs, hands, or feet, forming hard lumps—painful or painless—with no flush of blood upon pressure. Even if slightly red, the color is pale and cool, hard as stone. When broken, there is no pus, only thin blood, clear fluid, or milky liquid; sometimes, spoiled flesh resembles torn cotton, or like scrofula nestled in the skin and flesh, resembling a parrot’s egg floating in water—movable, soft, and not hard. When pierced, no pus or blood emerges, and the wound gapes with protruding flesh. The only sensation is phlegm obstructing the throat, with alternating chills and fever—these are its signs. Urgently add *nanxing* and *banxia* to this formula to treat internally, and externally apply *Yulong* hot medicine to draw out the toxin, allowing it to form pus and rupture for the best outcome. In milder cases without pus, it will resolve internally. If heat is extreme and phlegm congested, use *Kongxian Dan*—equal parts *zidaji*, *gansui*, and *baijiezi*—ground into powder and made into pills with rice paste.

If hard lumps the size of cups or bowls appear in critical areas like the throat or neck, the condition is especially difficult to treat. When blood and qi flow harmoniously, no other illness arises; when qi flows improperly, blood transforms into phlegm. If phlegm further loses its path, blood and qi decline and fail to form pus, only producing swelling and hardness—this is inevitable. This syndrome involves more yin than yang, so treat according to the pattern: restore yang, generate qi, supplement blood, and expel phlegm. Externally, use subsequent methods to rouse the body’s qi and blood and disperse cold lumps. If swelling does not subside, causes no pain, generates no heat, the body’s qi is robust, and no other symptoms appear, yet the flesh lump is indistinguishable from healthy tissue—this is another condition. Under no circumstances should one rashly use needles or blades to harm oneself. If a quack has already used needles, moxibustion, or corrosive medicines to rot the flesh, causing alternating chills and high fever, the proper approach is to harmonize and unblock qi and blood. Add *shengma* to this formula to dispel cold evil, and use wound-closing, scab-forming medicines to stabilize it, reducing it to a mere wart or growth. If the disease itself festers and rots, unavoidably requiring a blade, there is a marvelous preparation that can replace the knife—do not casually reveal it: mix *baifan* and *kupohua* into powder and apply it.

For intestinal abscesses within the abdomen, take *Shixuan San* alternately with this formula, adding *rendongteng*, which is most effective for internal abscesses. One must carefully assess deficiency and excess—whether to unblock or supplement. For supplementation, use *fuzi*; for unblocking, use *dahuang*. If deficiency and excess are unclear, this formula naturally harmonizes and unblocks, while *Shixuan* inherently supplements internally, preventing other complications.

For lung abscesses, the first signs are difficulty eating and slight pain in the chest and diaphragm—this is the condition. Immediately examine the pulse and assess deficiency or excess. If deficient, use this formula with *fuzi*, adjusting as needed. If the condition worsens slightly, use *Shixuan San* to supplement internally, and it will resolve. If excess, use this formula with *dahuang* to slightly unblock, allowing the toxic qi to descend—this is optimal. The lung and large intestine are exterior-interior related. If an internal abscess has already formed, combine *Haishang Fang* with this formula, adjusting as needed.

Wheezing, coughing, and expectorating pus and blood indicate a lung abscess. If pus exits through the navel in the stool, it is an abdominal abscess.

**Honeysuckle vine and licorice nodes boiled in wine are excellent.**

*End of Volume One of the Immortal-Transmitted Secret External Medicine Formulas*

**Volume Two of the Immortal-Transmitted Secret External Medicine Formulas**
*Compiled by Zhao Yizhen, the Original Yang Master of Junyi*

**Category: Warm Medicinal Formulas for Application**

**#3: Harmonizing Immortal Paste (also called Yellow Cloud Paste or Immortal Paste)**
*Used for conditions where cold and heat are indistinct; may be mixed with tea or wine according to the syndrome.*

- **Sichuan red-barked catalpa** (*chuan zi ke pi*), 5 *liang* in weight, stir-fried (also called *red flesh*)
  - *Also called* "internal disperser"
- **Red peony root** (*chi shao yao*), 2 *liang*, stir-fried
- **Pubescent angelica root** (*du huo*), 3 *liang*, stir-fried (do not use the nodes)
- **Dahurian angelica root** (*bai zhi*), 1 *liang* (do not expose to fire)
- **Wood wax** (*mu la*), also called *wang jian xiao* or *yang chun xue* (adjust according to need), stir-fried (this is *shi chang pu*, *Acorus tatarinowii*)

Grind the above five ingredients into a fine powder; detailed instructions for use follow.

All abscesses, deep-rooted ulcers, *liuzhu* (migratory abscesses), and miscellaneous diseases arise from stagnation of *qi* and blood. When exposed to warmth, they live; when exposed to cold, they die. Living, they disperse; dying, they congeal. This formula is warm and balanced.

- **Red-barked catalpa** is the essence of wood; it breaks *qi*, dispels blood, and resolves swelling.
- **Pubescent angelica root** is the essence of earth; it arrests wind, mobilizes blood, draws out bone toxins, dispels numbness and dampness, and, together with wood wax, breaks stony hardness.
- **Red peony root** is the essence of fire; it mildly generates blood, relieves pain, and dispels wind.
- **Wood wax** is the essence of water; it generates blood, relieves pain, resolves swelling, breaks wind, and disperses blood.
- **Dahurian angelica root** is the essence of metal; it dispels wind, generates flesh, and relieves pain.

When blood is generated, it does not die; when blood moves, it flows freely. When flesh is generated, it does not rot; when pain ceases, it does not fester. When wind departs, blood naturally disperses; when *qi* is broken, hardness dissolves, and toxins scatter. With these five agents working in concert, how could the disease fail to be cured?

---

All diseases manifest in three syndromes, and treatment employs three methods.

- If the disease is extremely hot, double the amounts of red-barked catalpa and wood wax in this formula, and slightly reduce the other three ingredients. It will still disperse, though more slowly.
- If the disease is extremely cold, slightly increase red peony root and pubescent angelica root; this will activate blood and disperse the condition, though the effect will be somewhat delayed without harming the disease.

---

- If the disease’s heat is overwhelming, do not use wine to mix the medicine. Instead, steep scallions in hot water to prepare a decoction, then mix the medicine and apply it warm. Scallions also disperse *qi*, and blood flows when warmed—hence the use of warm application.
- Once the disease has slightly abated, return to using wine to mix the medicine. Wine generates blood, and when warmed, blood is further generated. Wine also mobilizes blood, and when warmed, blood flows more freely.

---

- If the sore surface develops blood blisters or small sores, do not use wood wax, as its drying nature may cause discomfort when the medicine is applied. First apply the other four ingredients, then cover with wood wax to encircle and cut off the attacking blood pathways.
- When applying medicine, always apply it warm. If it dries, moisten it again with the original decoction to keep the medicinal properties damp and steaming, allowing the disease to recede.

---

- If, when using the standard formula, the black halo around the sore does not recede and the sore’s opening lacks blood color, it indicates the patient has previously used excessive cold medicine. Do not immediately apply *Yulong* (Jade Dragon), as the flesh is not yet dead, and the strong medicinal force may increase suffering. Instead, add cinnamon bark (*rou gui*) and Chinese angelica (*dang gui*) to this formula to revive the dead blood. The black halo will naturally recede, and efficacy will appear. Once the blood returns, discontinue the additions and use only the standard formula to achieve results.

---

- If pain persists when using the standard formula, dissolve frankincense (*ru xiang*) and myrrh (*mo yao*) in wine over a fire, then mix this wine with the medicine and apply it warm to relieve pain.

---

- For *liuzhu* (migratory abscesses) where the sinews fail to extend, add frankincense to this formula and apply it; its nature promotes sinew extension.

---

- If the sore’s opening develops protruding red flesh, there are three possible causes: (1) exposure to water, (2) exposure to wind, or (3) inversion of the wound edges after a knife incision.
  - Add a small amount of *arisaema* (*nan xing*) to dispel wind, and mix the medicine with ginger juice and wine.
  - If it does not resolve, the condition likely arose because an unskilled physician pressed out the pus core too forcefully and then applied cold medicine to the skin, causing this outcome. If hot medicine is applied, it will further rot the flesh. There is a secret method for this: apply a powder of alum (*bai fan*) and dried ginger (*ku po xiao*), then dust with sulfur (*liu huang*). Internally, administer *Rong Wei Jia Dui Jin Yin* (Nourishing and Defending Decoction with Paired Metal), and externally apply *Chong He* (Harmonizing Immortal Paste).

---

- If the disease’s heat is severe, do not immediately use cold medicine. Excessive heat causes *qi* and blood to congest, and extreme cold causes blood to retreat incompletely, congealing due to cold. Thus, use a balanced combination of warm and cool. Add *Dui Ting Hong Bao Dan* (Balanced Treasure Elixir) to this formula, mix with scallion decoction, and apply.

---

This formula is the foremost medicine for *fabei* (carbuncles of the back) and *liuzhu* (migratory abscesses). Scholars should master its flexible application, aligning internal and external treatments so that the disease remains within their grasp.

However, severe *fabei* is a matter of life and death, and only this formula offers the most stable and reliable efficacy from beginning to end, with no risk of deterioration. In mild cases, even folk healers may achieve results, though complications and harmful consequences may arise. This is because *fabei* arising from *yin* cannot be treated by folk healers. How could any other formula compare to this one, which treats both *yin* and *yang* conditions and seizes the divine power of creation?

As for *liuzhu*, though it is not fatal, nine out of ten cases result in disability. Prolonged disability leads to death, and even if a physician can cure it, recovery typically takes half a year to a full year.

This occurs in but one out of a hundred cases—yet the body remains feeble and impaired. Only the immortal formulas of our lineage, with their extraordinary and swift efficacy, never fail even once, possessing the power to snatch life from the jaws of death—effects beyond full description in words.

Now, *liuzhu* arises from residual toxins of *shanghan*. When the exterior has not fully resolved, these toxins lodge in the channels and network vessels. If qi and blood become uneven, they transform into *heat-liuzhu*. Those cases physicians can cure are the heat type. Heat diseases rarely involve excessive exterior dispersion, but when qi and blood are depleted, residual toxins flow into the interstices—whether loose or tight—and become *cold-liuzhu*. These are the cases physicians find difficult to cure.

Cold diseases are far more common. Thus, when *shanghan*’s exterior has not fully resolved, it is not merely a heat pattern; its residual toxins often manifest as cold patterns, leading to cold paralysis. When rooted in kidney deficiency, it produces *guju*. Cold causes qi to stagnate further and blood to congeal, resulting only in swelling without suppuration. If a physician administers cold formulas, they "cool what is already cold," and *yin* dies within *yin*, leading only to putrefying flesh, bone-bound toxins, and *guying*—ultimately, disability. Thus, *guying* is the failed outcome of *liuzhu*.

Yet it is said: *guying* is not the fault of *liuzhu*, but the error of physicians who prescribe cooling remedies. For *liu* means movement, and *zhu* means stagnation.

When qi flows and stagnates, blood congeals where it settles. Qi is yang; blood is yin. When yang moves, yin follows; when qi circulates, blood flows. The reason we can shift *liuzhu* to another location and disperse it lies precisely in its capacity for movement. Where movement occurs, displacement follows—once yang has moved and stirred, how can yin remain stagnant and not follow? Thus, we employ *duhuo* to guide it, for its nature is to stir and agitate both qi and blood. Once guided into motion, yin and yang harmonize, preventing suppuration and dispersing the condition at the new site—an inevitable outcome.

When *liuzhu* arises in critical areas such as the back, shoulders, waist, or legs, apply this formula thickly to the affected site. Then, use *duhuo* powder alone, mixed with wine and applied hot along a single path, leading to its endpoint where *yulong* is applied—this is the method of displacement. It directs the blood and qi to another location, causing them to gather and suppurate in a non-critical area. Alternatively, it may be resolved entirely. If pus has already formed, displacement is no longer possible; instead, apply this medicine urgently to draw out the toxins and prevent the development of *guying*. When incompetent physicians use cooling remedies or improperly employ needles and blades, *guying* forms—a condition not curable by medicine, requiring only that the decayed bone be expelled before healing can occur. If one hesitates to use needles or blades to extract it, apply *yulong*; the treatment method follows later. If the primary bone is expelled, no remedy exists; if a secondary bone is expelled, recovery is possible.

One formula uses *baizhi* and *zijinpi*, mixed with wine, to internally resolve early-stage abscesses—named *Yisheng Gao*. Another formula employs *chishaoyao*, *mola*, and *zishengpi* as a binding medicine—named *Sansheng Gao*. Another method treats adults or children who have accidentally severed their tongues with a blade, where the tongue hangs but remains partially attached: wrap the tongue in the soft inner membrane of a chicken egg, apply *Poxue Dan* mixed with honey to the root of the tongue to stanch bleeding, then blend honey and wax to an appropriate consistency, incorporating this primary formula. Apply it to the chicken membrane, as its softness and thinness allow the medicine to penetrate. If the tongue remains in the mouth, frequently reapply the melted mixture. After three days, the tongue will reattach, at which point the chicken membrane may be removed, and only the honey-wax mixture with the medicine need be applied diligently. Full recovery occurs within seven days. Those who study this will understand the marvelous applications of adaptable methods, which do not rely solely on the transmission of a teacher. If rapid results are not achieved, combine with *jinchuang* remedies for even greater efficacy.

For abscesses that have not yet formed pus, do not immediately apply *Hongbao Dan* to the head of the swelling, lest the cold medicine freeze the blood, preventing resolution and suppuration while causing flesh to rot. Instead, apply this formula. If the swelling does not resolve and suppuration is desired, apply *yulong* to the head of the abscess to dry it. Then, place this primary formula beneath the *yulong*, encircling the abscess with *Hongbao Dan* to block the influx of fresh blood. If the condition has not been excessively chilled by cooling medicines, there is no need to apply this formula beneath the *yulong*; simply encircle the abscess with *Hongbao Dan*.

When treating severe or deteriorating conditions, if *yulong* cannot yet be applied, use this formula alone for stability and to spare the patient distress. For nascent *fabei* that has not yet formed, apply *zijinpi* powder mixed with wine as a binding agent to naturally constrict and prevent spreading. Internally, administer *Zuomu Yinzi*—an excellent remedy for the impoverished.

Adding two-thirds parts *nanxing* and *caowu* to this formula, mixed with hot wine and applied, can cause any abscess to suppurate painlessly. Using *yulong* alone requires *Hongbao Dan* to encircle it, but this method is superior. For rabid dog bites, apply *zijiao* mixed with brown sugar, leaving the wound open. Use *jindan* to reduce swelling, and place chewed apricot kernels in the wound to draw out toxins.

Another method combines equal parts *nanxing* and *caowu* with this formula, mixed with hot wine and applied, to treat long-standing injuries—most effective. For soft nodules in children, add *junjiang* to this formula, mixed with wine and applied. If applied at the onset, this formula alone, mixed with wine, will suffice until pus forms. At the initial stage, applying *zijinpi* and *mola* mixed with wine can ensure resolution; under no circumstances should *Hongbao Dan* be used.

*Biaoju* arises from heart-fire and heat toxins, manifesting in the five centers with unbearable pain. Its appearance resembles blistering sores with red blood, and though its external form is small, the internal heat toxin is severe. If located in the heart or diaphragm, it is difficult to treat; if in the palms or soles, it may be cured. However, treatment must be prompt—delay or localization in the heart or diaphragm leads to putrefied, pulverized flesh, beyond even the aid of immortals. For this condition in the palm, apply *Hongbao Dan* in a ring around the palm to block the blood, then use *Zhonghe* at the center, leaving an opening to complete the treatment. For the sole, apply *Hongbao* along the foot’s meridian at the *jiaogu* bone, encircling an area one to two *cun* in length, then use *Chonghe* as before to conclude. *Xianchuan Waike Mifang*, Scroll Two, concludes.

**Scroll Three of *Xianchuan Waike Mifang*
Compiled by Zhao Yizhen of Junyi Yuanchang**

**Section Four: Topical Applications of Heating Medicines**

**Huanyang Yulong Ointment (Yang-Returning Jade Dragon Ointment), Hot in Nature**
- *Cao Wu* (wild aconite), 3 *liang*, stir-fried
- *Nan Xing* (arisaema), 1 *liang*, roasted
- *Jun Jiang* (ginger), 2 *liang*, roasted
- *Bai Zhi* (angelica), 1 *liang*, unheated
- *Chi Shao Yao* (red peony), 1 *liang*, stir-fried
- *Rou Gui* (cassia bark), ½ *liang*, unheated

This formula treats yin-type *fabei* (back carbuncles), cold *liuzhu* (flowing phlegm), *guzhui* wind (drumstick wind), chronic traumatic pain, cold impediment with wind-damp, all cases of cold leg qi with swelling but no redness, cold pain without swelling, numbness of the feet, and cold blood wind in women—it is the foremost medicine for all yin patterns.

**Usage** is detailed below; apply mixed with warm wine.

Though miscellaneous diseases manifest in the skin, hands, or feet, their root necessarily lies in the five viscera and six bowels. The blood vessels and meridians of the viscera and bowels circulate throughout the body day and night, completing their cycle without cease. When one viscus is afflicted, the disease must appear where its pulse and qi traverse, corresponding to the yin-yang divisions of the hands and feet. Diseases may be cold or hot: hot ones are easily treated, while cold ones are difficult to cure. Cold necessarily arises from deficiency of original yang in the viscera and bowels, allowing wind evil to exploit the gap and enter. When qi and blood become uneven, the evil travels from the meridians to lodge in the skin, obstructing the smooth flow of the vessels. The more cold congeals, the more it accumulates without dispersing. If incompetent physicians then apply freezing agents, attacking both internally and externally, the condition rarely fails to become critical. The scholar must observe the external signs while examining their internal correspondences; when exterior and interior align, not a single case will be missed.

This medicine contains *Jun Jiang* and *Rou Gui*, sufficient to warm the blood and generate blood. Yet once generated and warmed, if it cannot disperse, it may instead cause harm. Thus, *Cao Wu* and *Nan Xing* are included to break evil qi, expel wind toxin, enliven dead flesh, relieve bone pain, dissolve lumps, and summon yang qi. *Chi Shao* and *Bai Zhi* further disperse stagnant blood, alleviate suffering, and regenerate flesh. Combined with wine to circulate the medicine’s nature and disperse qi and blood, even the most severe cold patterns will be cured—like kindling flame from cold ashes or reviving spring in withered wood.

Generally, cold diseases cause yin decay of flesh, rendering it insensate to pain or itching. If pain is present, it often clings to the bone; left untreated, the cold root penetrates the marrow, beyond the reach of ordinary medicines. Only this formula can thoroughly expel yin toxin, restore yang qi, relieve bone pain, and halt diseases of the flesh and skin—this much is evident. However, its use must be measured; excess should be avoided to achieve perfection. Variations in treatment are outlined below.

1. **Yin-type *fabei* (back carbuncle)**, or cases misdiagnosed with cold medicines, or originally yang-type *fabei* mistakenly treated with cold medicines—where yang transforms into yin, the entire back blackens and decays. Apply *Hongbao Dan* around the healthy flesh at the edges to contain the blood, then apply this ointment to the center. After one night, yang qi will return, and blackened areas will turn red. Once redness and vitality are observed, discontinue this medicine and use *Chonghe* to complete the treatment. If ineffective and suppuration begins, add *Nan Xing* and *Cao Wu* to *Chonghe* for use. If yang has returned and blackness has turned red, but a single blackened, decayed spot remains that refuses to redden—this indicates dead blood. Apply *Pu Xiao* (mirabilite) and *Ming Fan* (alum), or alternatively, *Bai Dingxiang* (sparrow droppings), *Peng Sha* (borax), and *Ru Xiang* (frankincense), mixed with saliva. Draw a circle at the junction of black and red, then cover with *Chonghe*. By the next morning, the blackened flesh will detach as if cut away. Wash the area, apply flesh-generating medicine, and close the wound to complete treatment.

2. **Cold *liuzhu* (flowing phlegm)** often clings to the bone, with hardened flesh that does not resolve, cold bone pain, and contracted sinews that cannot extend. Light use of the knife or needle yields no pus or blood—only thin, watery discharge or static blood. Apply this ointment. If slightly delayed, mix equal parts *Jun Jiang*, *Bai Zhi*, *Rou Gui*, and *Cao Wu* with warm wine for application. Once bone cold is dispelled and pain ceases, qi will warm and harmonize, sinews will naturally extend, and hardened flesh will resolve. However, treating *liuzhu* cannot omit *Mu La* (litsea), as its nature excels at breaking stagnant qi and dispersing hard swellings. Yet it must not be used in excess, for it can neutralize the medicine’s nature—this pattern fundamentally relies on warming medicines.

3. **Guzhui wind (drumstick wind)** arises from internal dampness, residual toxin from cold damage, complications of *liuzhu*, or wind-damp vacuity impediment. This condition has three presentations:
   - The knees knock together when walking, with trembling steps and slight swelling along the knee’s *jinggu* bone.
   - The junction of the knee’s *jinggu* bone swells to the size of a mallet, the thigh flesh wastes away, and the skin contracts around the bone.
   - The upper leg swells while the lower leg cools and wastes.

   The feet and knees pertain to the liver. When the liver meridian is invaded by wind, cold, and damp qi, blood and vessels fail to flow, giving rise to this condition. The knees become cold and obstructed, congealing and stagnating. The lower leg’s vessels have outflow but no return, growing ever thinner, colder, and more contracted. The upper leg’s vessels accumulate without dispersion, swelling while the flesh wastes and grows hotter. If originating from *liuzhu*, the flesh may become numb and decay; decay leads to cold toxin corroding the bone. Once the bone rots and emerges, even immortals have no remedy.

Before ulceration occurs and the flesh has not yet necrosed, promptly apply this medicinal paste—mixed with hot wine—to the upper leg at the knee and *jinggu* bone to arrest bone pain and restore yang qi. Then apply *Chonghe* ointment to the cold lower leg to guide the blood and qi, promoting downward flow and unblocking the vessels. Apply this formula again to the junction of the meridian and bone to connect the guided vessels and disperse the accumulated yin qi. Internally, administer *Zhuifeng* pills with double the usual dose of frankincense to relax the sinews, following the prescribed method—none will fail to recover.

If the patient wishes to go out, one may use *Wuji* powder with added ginger, cinnamon, angelica, and *bai zhi*, further augmented with large Sichuan aconite, *niu xi*, areca nut, and chaenomeles, mixed with tea or wine. For men or women suffering long from cold impediment, blood wind, numbness, or inability to move the limbs, fill a cotton pouch with this medicine and bind it lengthwise around the painful area, securing it with a silk bag. This medicine relieves bone pain; when applied to the flesh, the skin will feel as if ants are crawling—this signals its efficacy. For impediment, add equal parts *ding pi*, evodia fruit, myrrh, and large Sichuan aconite, then combine with *Zhuifeng* pills for simultaneous internal and external treatment—its curative power is divine.

For unbearable wind-leg pain, take *Zhuifeng* pills internally and apply this formula externally, mixed with raw flour and ginger juice as a hot poultice. For immediate relief, add frankincense and myrrh, dissolving them in wine for application—this is most effective.

For long-standing injuries penetrating the bone—caused by falls, blows, or fractures where blood was not properly circulated—stagnant blood lingers at the site of injury. Over time, it adheres like chicken lungs to ribs, or in milder cases, like moss clinging to stone. In youth, when blood and qi are warm and harmonious, one may scarcely notice; but in old age, when blood declines, exposure to wind, cold, rain, or dampness triggers the ailment. Apply this formula mixed with hot wine, and internally administer *Sousun Xuntong* pills—combined internal and external treatment is most effective. However, in those with deficient blood and qi, if the illness resides between the chest, ribs, waist, or back, it is called "unremoved grime," which may transform into consumptive blood stasis. Regardless of age or duration—whether affecting the entire body or just a fist or elbow—the treatment is the same. Such conditions are deeply rooted; they cannot be cured with a single dose but require months or years to stabilize. Those not yet consumptive are easily healed; once consumptive, they are difficult.

One method uses *nan xing* and *cao wu* with a small amount of cinnamon to dispel blackened necrosis and purulent decay—this is called *Xiao Yulong*. For *shi yong* (stone-like carbuncles), apply this formula mixed with hot wine, then encircle the area with *Hongbao* paste to await suppuration and rupture.

For women with breast abscesses: these often arise after weaning when milk fails to recede; or when milk is abundant but the infant nurses little, causing stagnation and clumping; or when menstruation is irregular and flows counter to its proper course; or when pathogenic qi becomes internally constrained, later forming swollen abscesses. At the initial onset, *do not* use cooling medicines to freeze it. The breast is formed from transformed blood; if it cannot drain, it hardens into swollen lumps. Its nature is inherently cold—if chilled by cold medicines, it congeals and fails to disperse. Over time, external blood unable to transform into milk generates heat and pain, steaming the breast lumps into pus. The suffering is extraordinary, and the abscess will not cease until it rots completely. Thus, those who recover from breast abscesses lose their milk. The breast’s nature is most cold; when further obstructed by cooling agents, yin putrefaction is inevitable. However, cooling medicines are not entirely without use—they are beneficial *after* ulceration. At the initial onset, apply this formula with *nan xing*, mixed evenly with equal parts ginger juice and wine, as a hot poultice to dissolve it internally. For faster results, add *cao wu*—its potent nature can break up malignant masses, expel cold and heat, dissolve when cold, and suppurate when hot. If an abscess has already formed, use *Chonghe* as usual, or add *cao wu* and *nan xing*. After rupture, observe the cause: if from cold, use *Chonghe* to complete the cure; if from heat, use *Hongbao* to generate flesh. Additionally, apply frankincense and myrrh to relieve pain and reduce suffering. For internal medicine, use *Gualou* powder, adjusting for deficiency or excess with *Tongshun* powder or *Shixuan* decoction in alternation.

For multiple openings, it is called *ru fa* (breast effusion); for hardened breast lumps, *ru shi* (breast stone); for swelling at the nipple, *chui ru* (blown breast); for abscesses below the breast pouch, *ru lou* (breast fistula)—the latter is difficult to treat due to the pendulous flesh where blood readily pools. A single opening is *ru yong* (breast abscess); for those over fifty, there is no treatment. However, *laoren ru jie* (elderly breast nodes) may still be treated, as abscesses form on the pendulous flesh, while nodes appear near the armpit.

For chronic phlegm deviating from its path, causing swollen abscesses without pus, apply this medicine to induce a response—the illness will discharge laterally, allowing for a better outcome. Otherwise, if original yang is depleted, this becomes a critical condition. For such critical depletion of original yang, urgently apply *Quanti Yulong* to draw out pus, and take *Tongshun* powder with added platycodon, pinellia, tangkuei, and cinnamon. If the illness is red, lively, and rapidly heating, *Danggui Chonghe* is best—*never* mistakenly administer freezing agents. This formula only draws toxins to form pus; discontinue once the illness recedes, and do not overuse. Combining it with *Daomai Shenji* is even more effective, as recorded in *Waike Jingyao*.

For abdominal abscesses, nine out of ten cases are fatal. The stomach pertains to yin, and external cold envelops internal heat. When qi and blood converge, they seek heat and avoid cold, often forming internal abscesses that cannot manifest externally. Occasionally, a faint shadow may appear, but if touched by cold medicines or cooling agents, even immortals cannot apply their skill—how can physicians not be cautious?

For any case presenting these symptoms, if at the onset there is abdominal pain and, upon pressing with the hand, the pain shifts and moves, this indicates a *qi* mass. If the root remains fixed and there is slight redness and swelling externally, it signifies an internal abscess. Immediately apply this formula to draw out the toxic *qi* and transform it into an external abscess, then complete the treatment with *Chonghe*. Internally, administer *Tongshun* powder with added *rendongteng*, following the method described previously. If the abscess naturally manifests externally, there is no need for this formula—simply use *Chonghe*, and avoid reckless use of needles or blades. Should the abscess be incised with metal instruments, causing the wound to gape, apply *Yulong* to the head of the abscess and encircle the edges with *Chonghe*, allowing it to rupture naturally. If the pus does not drain swiftly, mix three parts *Hongbao* with seven parts ginger juice and tea, then apply to facilitate complete drainage. Internally, use *Shixuan* to nourish and regenerate flesh, while externally, continue with *Chonghe* to conclude the treatment. This condition involves excess yin and deficient yang, inflicting severe harm upon the patient. As recovery nears, double the dosage of internal tonics to restore *qi* and blood, thereby promoting easier healing; otherwise, the abscess may recede only to swell again, and the wound may fail to close. Even after stabilization, it is advisable to take internal tonics with added *fuzi* to prevent *qi* deficiency from impeding recovery.

In cold-type cases, it is especially important to administer *fuzi* before the abscess ruptures; however, after rupture, avoid hasty astringent or wound-sealing medicines, lest residual toxins remain trapped. With proper medicinal treatment, the wound will close naturally. If the internal abscess has already formed and cannot be drawn outward, apply *Chonghe* externally to warm and promote suppuration, allowing the pus to discharge from the viscera rather than causing internal putrefaction. Life and death hinge entirely on the efficacy of the medicinal treatment—refer to the methods outlined earlier. Most critically, avoid toxic foods, as their consumption will provoke recurrence, making the condition refractory and difficult to cure.

For pregnant women afflicted with this condition, the approach differs: internally, administer *Zisu* decoction to calm the fetus, and avoid other medicines unless absolutely necessary. If the condition arises near term, the child and pus will descend together; if the pregnancy is still distant, the pus will discharge through the large intestine. If detected early, medicinal treatment alone may resolve it. Should the abscess manifest externally, the condition will necessarily involve heat, and *Chonghe* alone should be used to conclude the treatment—though its application must be carefully weighed against the severity of the case to avoid error.

*Concluding Volume Three of the Immortal’s Transmitted External and Secret Formulas*
*Beginning Volume Four of the Immortal’s Transmitted External and Secret Formulas*

**Hongbao Elixir (also known as Golden Elixir, Inch of Gold, Four Yellows Powder, One Yellow Powder, Blood-Breaking Elixir, or Yellow Medicine)**

**Ingredients:**
- *Tianhuafen* (Trichosanthes root) – 3 *liang*
- *Jianghuang* (turmeric) – 1 *liang*
- *Baizhi* (angelica root) – 1 *liang*
- *Chishaoyao* (red peony root) – 2 *liang*

Grind into a fine powder. Use tea, wine, or water as a vehicle, applying topically for all heat-type abscesses, swellings, and metal-inflicted wounds.

This medicine is purely cooling. It can transform blood into water yet also cause blood stasis; it cools the flesh and promotes flesh regeneration while removing dead or rotting tissue. It breaks up blood and reduces swelling, yet may also cause qi to stagnate and rise. It can stop pain or induce it, close pus or expel it—its effects are contradictory. The formula’s nature is simple: its efficacy is slight when applied to cold conditions but pronounced when used for heat. Thus, it must not be used lightly for non-severe yang patterns, lest it congeal cold and complicate treatment.

For metal-inflicted wounds with bleeding, nothing surpasses this remedy—it is the foremost medicine. In other cases, it serves merely as an auxiliary to the two preceding formulas. Discernment is paramount.

Generally, these three medicines may:
- Combine forces for a shared effect,
- Operate independently with specialized authority,
- Divide duties in coordinated roles,
- Encircle and attack in concert,
- Lend mutual reinforcement,
- Engage in stalwart resistance,
- Serve as the vanguard with orthodox tactics or achieve victory through unorthodox means,
- Or lead with unorthodox tactics and secure triumph with orthodox methods.

Their ingenuity and precision mirror the art of warfare. Yet as troops follow the seal and generals obey commands, success hinges on the practitioner. Without one who understands principles and adapts to change, what can be said of mastery?

**Methods of Application:**

1. For severe heat in the condition, mix with hot tea and apply.
   For milder heat, use wine as the vehicle.
   To draw out pus, blend with three parts ginger juice and seven parts tea. Why? This medicine is profoundly cooling and causes blood to recede, while ginger juice, being warm, draws blood forward. Thus, blood retreats and is then propelled, creating a dynamic tension that matures the pus and prompts its discharge.

2. If the wound’s edges harden and fail to resolve, the area has been assailed by wind. Add *duhuo* (angelica pubescens) to this formula and mix with hot wine. If improvement remains elusive, the wind-toxin has penetrated deeply, and the flesh has hardened. Add *zijingpi* (bark of the redbud tree), and resolution is assured.

3. This formula excels at treating metal wounds and heat-type red swellings, staunching the root of bleeding to prevent redness and protrusion. However, it must not be used lightly for carbuncles or deep abscesses, lest the toxin fail to disperse and instead penetrate inward. If it lodges in the bone, it may form a bone abscess; in the throat, it may gather as toxic qi; in the chest or back, it may rot the viscera; in the abdomen, it may become an internal abscess—leading to irreversible harm. One must exercise utmost caution. For such cases, *Chonghe* and *Yulong* should be used according to the method and symptoms.

4. For the young with robust blood or the elderly with debilitated blood, if bleeding cannot be stopped, the patient may perish as blood is exhausted. If the wound is on the limbs, apply the medicine mixed with tea to the area a *chi* above and below the injury. If on the chest, back, waist, or abdomen, cover the entire body to block the blood’s path. Then administer *Duanxue* powder (fivefold the usual dose) or the *Divine Military Formula* (see later) to the wound, and healing will follow.

5. For severe metal wounds where sinews are severed, pulses cut off, and blood lost to the point of death, to staunch bleeding, first bind the arm with a cord or silk sash. Then apply this formula mixed with tea to the arm to block the blood’s path. Next, use the *Duanxue* medicine on the wound. Avoid internal tonics such as *Siwu* decoction, as they may induce vomiting or, in severe cases, cause facial distortion, restlessness, fever, and tetanus. Instead, administer the *Duijin* drink with *Chuanxiong* and *Baizhi*, decocted with ginger and jujube, to stabilize the condition, followed by gradual blood supplementation. If tetanus develops, use tetanus-specific medicines such as *Gegen* decoction (see later). For the wound, apply the *Military Formula* with nine-lobed turtle shell (stir-fried with suet and ground).

6. For metal wounds on the head or face where bleeding does not cease, urgently apply this formula mixed with tea in a circular band around the neck to block the blood. Also apply it to the wound’s edges, and use the *Military Formula* on the wound itself. Severe cases will resolve in ten days; mild ones in three.

7. For metal wounds exposed to water, where the flesh turns white and blooms like a flower, mix this formula with scallion juice and apply to both sides of the wound, then warm gently with fire. Alternatively, fumigate with smoke from early-season rice stalks. If water exudes from the wound, healing will follow. If no water appears, wind has invaded—apply *nanxing* (arisaema) mixed with tea, and the condition will resolve. Then use the *Military Formula* on the wound.

8. For women postpartum or during menopause with reversed blood flow, where the heart cannot govern and blood is vomited, or epistaxis or tongue bleeding occurs, apply this formula mixed with well-water or fresh mugwort juice to the neck. The bleeding will cease immediately, after which internal medicine should be given to address the root. For tongue bleeding, if blood blisters form and refill upon bursting, tie a thread around the base of the tongue (do not remove it). Apply the medicine to the neck to block the blood, and internally administer heart-cooling medicines such as *Huangqin* and *Jingjie*. For the tongue, use honey-mixed wound-sealing medicine. Once the blister is lanced and the thread removed, the bleeding will not recur. Follow with heart-cooling medicines: *Siwu* decoction with *Jingjie*, *Bohe*, and *Zhusha*.

9. When applying this formula to hot toxins, if the area dries and pain persists with redness and swelling, mix with egg white for application. This method is wondrous for stubborn heat toxins.

For scalds and burns, the treatment is the same.

If injuries from blows or falls occur on the chest or diaphragm, and the medicine fails to promote blood flow downward, mix this medicinal powder with mung bean water and swallow it to induce vomiting, after which the patient will recover.

There are also cases where, after a fall from a height, blood-flow-promoting medicines fail to take effect, and after several days the patient nears death. This is invariably due to cold weather freezing the blood after administering *Dahuang* and similar medicines, causing the blood to congeal and stagnate. In such cases, mix *Junjiang* powder with hot wine and administer it. Within moments, the blood will flow freely, and the patient will revive. This method relies on the heat of the wine to activate stagnant blood, allowing the previous medicines to take effect.

**Formulas to be Used Together, Section Six**

**Huangfan Pill (also called Humo Powder)**
- *Mingfan* (raw alum), one *liang*, ground to powder
- Yellow beeswax

Melt the beeswax over a fire, then remove it and allow it to cool to ninety percent. Pour the alum powder into the wax and form into pills the size of *wutong* seeds. Take ten pills per dose, increasing to twenty, with rice water. If the sore has not yet ulcerated, it will soon rupture; if already ulcerated, it will close. This formula powerfully protects the membranes and rescues the heart, preventing toxins from attacking internally.

**Zhamu Drink**
Treats carbuncles and deep-rooted boils on the back, whether formed or forming. This is an excellent formula for the poor.
- Dried *zhamu* leaves, four *liang*
- Dried lotus leaf hearts and stems
- *Xuancaogen*
- *Gancao* nodes
- *Diyu*, each one *liang*

Grind into a powder. For each dose, take half a *liang*, decoct in two bowls of water until one bowl remains, and divide into two servings. Take one in the morning and one in the evening, then decoct the dregs again. If pus is present, it will dry; if pus is forming, it will disperse. Avoid all toxic foods.

**Sanshi Powder**
Treats sores with wasting thirst and frequent urination.
- *Renshen*, one *qian*
- *Baimu*, one *qian*
- *Danggui*, one *qian*
- *Baishaoyao*, one *qian*
- *Kujie*, one *qian*
- *Zhimu*, one *qian*
- *Shanzhizi*, one *qian*
- *Fuling*, two *qian*
- *Lianqiao*, two *qian*
- *Tianhuafen*, two *qian*
- *Gange*, two *qian*
- *Rougui*, half a *qian*
- *Huoxiang*, half a *qian*
- *Muxiang*, half a *qian*
- *Gancao*, six *qian*
- *Poxiao*, one *liang* and six *qian*
- *Hanshuishi*, eight *qian*
- *Shigao*, eight *qian*
- *Huashi*, one *liang*
- *Dahuang*, one *liang*

Grind into a powder. For each dose, take five *qian*, decoct in one cup of water with three slices of ginger until half remains. Strain through cloth or silk, add a small amount of honey, and administer. Gradually increase to one *liang*, taking three doses per day to ensure smooth urination. If diarrhea occurs, omit *Poxiao* and *Dahuang*. The *Eight-Ingredient Pill* from *Waike Jingyao* also treats this condition.

**Gualou Powder**
Treats carbuncles and deep-rooted boils.
- *Gualou* (new or old), stir-fried with Sichuan peppercorns
- Sichuan peppercorns, twenty grains
- *Gancao*, three to five *cun*, sliced
- *Ruxiang*, five grains, the size of *zaojiao* seeds

Decoct in three bowls of weak wine until one bowl remains. Strain and take warm. The toxin will disperse immediately. If the sore has not yet formed, it will rupture; if already formed, pus will discharge without manual intervention.

**Haishang Formula**
Treats internal abscesses with pus, where putrid blood emits a foul stench, leading to cold pain in the navel and abdomen. This is caused by putrid pus, and this formula expels it.
- *Baizhi*, one *liang*
- *Baishaoyao*, half a *liang*
- *Baifan* (calcined), half a *liang*
- Single-leaf red *shukui* root, two *liang*

Grind into a powder, melt beeswax and alum, and form into pills the size of *wutong* seeds. Take thirty pills on an empty stomach before meals with rice water. Once the pus is fully expelled, supplement with *Shixuan Powder*.

**Alternative Formulas**
- Pig fat and crucian carp, simmered together, treat intestinal abscesses.
- *Biejia*, burned to retain its nature, and taken orally.

**Zhenjun Miaotie Powder**
- Bright sulfur, three *liang*
- Buckwheat flour, two *liang*

Grind into a powder, mix with well water to an appropriate consistency, and shape into cakes. Dry in the sun or over a fire and store. For malignant sores, grind again, mix with well water, and apply. If painful, it will cease; if painless, it will become painful before healing.

**Zhuifeng Pill**
Treats cold impediment, blood qi, numbness of the hands and feet, and wandering wind in the channels and network vessels leading to *guzhui* wind in both men and women.
- *Chenxiang*, five *qian*
- *Niuxi*, three *liang*, wine-soaked and stir-fried
- *Danggui*, three *liang*, baked
- *Yiyiren*, two *liang*
- *Baizhi*, two *liang*
- *Chuanxiong*, two *liang*
- *Qianghuo*, five *qian*
- *Fangfeng*, five *qian*, stir-fried
- *Chuanwu*, one piece, soaked
- *Chishaoyao*, five *qian*, stir-fried
- *Tianma*, five *qian*, stir-fried
- *Caowu*, five *qian*, stir-fried yellow
- *Rougui*, five *qian*
- *Ganjiang*, one *liang*, stir-fried
- *Dingpi*, five *qian*
- *Ruxiang*, five *qian*
- *Moyao*, five *qian*
- *Muxiang*, five *qian*
- *Mugua*, three *liang*, stir-fried

Grind into a powder and form into honey pills. Take thirty pills per dose with wine. For leg qi, form into pills with wine paste and take with warm wine. If using as a powder, mix with wine and administer. Avoid hot foods.

**Sousun Xuntong Pill**
Reconnects bones, treats generalized pain, and long-standing injuries reaching the bones. For blade wounds, use this formula afterward.
- *Ruxiang*, two *qian*
- *Moyao*, two *qian*
- *Danggui*, one *liang*
- *Junjiang*, five *qian*, stir-fried
- *Rougui*, three *qian*
- *Chuanxiong*, one *liang*
- *Yiyiren*, stir-fried, one *liang* (if sinews or pulses are severed, increase this ingredient)
- *Dingpi*, five *qian*
- *Duhuo*, stir-fried, five *qian*
- *Huixiang*, stir-fried, two *qian*
- *Caowu*, five *qian*, stir-fried yellow
- *Gusuibu*, stir-fried, two *liang*
- *Chishaoyao*, stir-fried, five *qian*
- *Baizhi*, stir-fried, five *qian*
- *Shiguteng*, stir-fried, five *qian* (some say two *liang*)

Grind into a powder and form into honey pills. Chew fresh ginger finely and swallow with warm wine. If using as a powder, mix with ginger wine and administer; it may also be steeped in wine. For fractures, apply the medicine only after generalized numbness occurs.

For bone knitting, add one *bi* of *caowu*, mix with ample hot wine, and administer according to the patient’s age, weakness, deficiency, or excess. If numbness persists, use a thick decoction of black soybeans to resolve it. If soybeans are unavailable, a strong brew of bland fermented black beans may suffice. If vomiting occurs, add ginger juice.

*Tongxue San* treats internal injuries without bleeding, bruising with redness and swelling, and constipation of urine and stool:
- *Dahuang*, three *qian*, wheat-wrapped and roasted

*Jiming San* also promotes blood circulation:
- *Dahuang*, raw powder
