Grind into a powder and administer.

**Formulas from Chancellor Bayan’s Military Manual**

**For treating all wounds from blades, arrows, and weapons (invariably effective):**
- *Ruxiang* (Frankincense)
- *Moyao* (Myrrh)
- *Qianghuo* (Notopterygium)
- *Zisu* (Perilla)
- *Xixin* (Asarum)
- *Wuyao* (Lindera) [alternatively, *Cao Wu* (Aconite)]
- *Shexiang* (Musk), half a *zi*
- *Shehanshi* (Serpentine), calcined
- *Hougui* (Cassia Bark)
- *Baizhi* (Angelica), unexposed to fire
- *Jiangxiang* (Dalbergia)
- *Danggui* (Chinese Angelica)
- *Sumu* (Sappan Wood)
- *Tanxiang* (Sandalwood)
- *Longgu* (Dragon Bone)
- *Nanxing* (Arisaema)
- *Liuhuang* (Sulfur)
- *Jishengwei* (Loranthus tips)
- *Huaruishi* (Ophicalcite), quenched in child’s urine ten times

Grind equal parts into powder and apply dry to wounds to stanch bleeding, relieve pain, dispel wind, and promote flesh regeneration. For the wound’s periphery, apply *Hongbao Dan* as a dressing—divinely efficacious.

**Fumigation and Wash Formula**
For all abscesses, deep-rooted boils, back carbuncles, traumatic injuries, fractures (whether bones are broken or merely swollen and painful), this formula treats all.

- *Sangbaipi* (Mulberry Root Bark), primary for traumatic wounds
- *Baizhi*, one and a half *liang*
- *Chishaoyao*, two *liang*
- *Wuyao* (Lindera), primary for swollen, painful bones
- *Zuochanteng* (Uncaria)
- *Jingjie* (Schizonepeta)
- *Juye* (Tangerine Leaf)
- *Huoxiang* (Patchouli), added for foul, rotting wounds
- *Jiuyegen* (Chinese Tallow Root) may also be used

Chop and decoct, adjusting ingredients as symptoms dictate. For each *liang* of herbs, use two bowls of water. For blade wounds, add *Jingjie* and *Sangbaipi*; for foulness, add *Huoxiang*; for toxic sores, add *Jiuyegen*. Wash with warm decoction from a jar. For severe, whole-body injuries, perform the treatment in a small, draft-free room: first heat several large bricks until red-hot, then fumigate and wash with the hot medicinal decoction. If the patient’s breath remains warm, repeatedly quench the red-hot bricks in the decoction to raise steam, inducing slight sweating for optimal effect.

**Shexiang Qingfen San (also called Taohong San)**
Promotes flesh regeneration, closes wounds, relieves pain, and dispels wind. For all abscesses, sores, fractures, or unhealed wounds, apply dry after washing.

- *Ruxiang* (Frankincense)
- *Moyao* (Myrrh)
- *Wubeizi* (Gallnut), baked—primary ingredient
- *Baizhi* (Angelica), unexposed to fire—dispels wind and promotes flesh regeneration
- *Chishaoyao* (Red Peony)—disperses blood stasis and relieves pain
- *Qingfen* (Calomel)
- *Guodan* (Lead Oxide), water-processed
- *Chishizhi* (Halloysite), calcined—quick-acting
- *Shexiang* (Musk)
- *Xuejie* (Dragon’s Blood)—stanches bleeding and promotes flesh regeneration
- *Binlang* (Areca)—stanches bleeding
- *Xuanlang* (Litsea)
- *Danggui* (Chinese Angelica), wine-washed and baked
- *Haipiaoxiao* (Cuttlefish Bone)

Grind into fine powder and apply to wounds.

**Shenyi Siqi Gao**
For all stubborn sores, malignant sores, and toxic sores that fail to heal.

- *Ruxiang* (Frankincense)
- *Moyao* (Myrrh)
- *Fangfeng* (Saposhnikovia)
- *Qianghuo* (Notopterygium)
- *Baizhi* (Angelica)
- *Chishaoyao* (Red Peony)
- *Danggui* (Chinese Angelica)
- *Xuanlian* (Coptis)
- *Rougui* (Cinnamon)
- *Zaojiao* (Gleditsia)
- *Wubeizi* (Gallnut)
- *Badou* (Croton), husks removed
- *Mubiezi* (Momordica)
- *Guodan* (Lead Oxide)
- *Bimazi* (Castor Bean)
- *Wumingyi* (Pyrolusite)
- *Binlang* (Areca)
- *Shuifen* (Processed Alum)
- *Qingfen* (Calomel)
- *Fengxiang* (Styrax)
- *Biba* (Long Pepper) [alternatively, *Wuyao* (Lindera)]
- *Songxiang* (Pine Resin)
- *Huangla* (Yellow Wax), equal parts
- *Taoliu Huaizhi* (Peach, Willow, and Sophora twigs)
- *La Gao* (Wax Paste)
- *Qingyou* (Refined Oil)

Except for *Ruxiang*, *Moyao*, *Shexiang*, *Qingfen*, and *Dan* (which are separately ground), first decoct all other ingredients in refined oil until charred, then add *Fengxiang*, *Songxiang*, *Huangla*, and *La Gao*, continuing to heat until dissolved. Strain through silk to remove dregs, then add *Guodan* and *Shuifen*, decocting until the mixture turns purple. Finally, incorporate the pre-ground *Ruxiang*, *Moyao*, *Shexiang*, and *Qingfen*, stirring constantly with the twigs until the paste holds its shape when dropped in water. Store in a ceramic vessel, allowing the fire toxin to dissipate before use.

**Another Formula for Stanching Bleeding, Promoting Flesh Regeneration, and Closing Wounds (adaptable for various uses):**
- *Huashi* (Talc), slow-acting
- *Hanshuishi* (Calcite), calcined
- *Shigao* (Gypsum), calcined—slow-acting
- *Fanxiang* (Burnt Borneol)
- *Xionghuang* (Realgar)—eliminates foulness and rot
- *Longgu* (Dragon Bone), calcined—quick-acting
- *Chuanshanjia* (Pangolin Scales), stir-fried to remove moisture
- *Baicaoshuang* (Soot)
- *Liujinu* (Artemisia), stir-fried—stanches bleeding
- *Wangbuliuxing* (Vaccaria), stir-fried—stanches bleeding
- *Jinyingzi* (Cherokee Rose Fruit)
- *Jiuliguang* (Senecio)—stanches bleeding
- *Zhugen* (Ramie Root), charred
- *Laosongpi* (Old Pine Bark), charred

Grind each into powder and use as needed.

**Zhutong Yi Hei San (also stanches bleeding):**
- *Baicaoshuang* (Soot)
- *Zhugen* (Ramie Root), charred
- *Fanjiang* (Burnt Ginger), charred

Apply to wounds.

First use *Laosongpi* (Old Pine Bark), charred and ground into powder, to treat blade wounds, caning injuries, and all unrelenting pain.

**Shenxiao Fuyuan Tongqi San**
For all malignant sores, taken at the first sign of onset (three doses in succession). Treats abscesses, deep-rooted boils, running sores, and painful swellings.

- *Danggui*, three *liang*
- *Gancao*, one *liang*
- *Shengdihuang*, half a *liang*
- *Huangqi*, one *liang*
- *Baishao*, one *liang*
- *Tianhuafen*, one *liang*
- *Shudihuang*, half a *liang*
- *Jinyinhua*, two *liang*

For each dose, use five *qian* of the formula, decoct in one and a half cups of water until one cup remains, and administer warm. Adjust timing based on the affected area (before or after meals).

**Alternative Names for Ingredients (present-day annotations):**
- *Mahanshi* (Horse Liver Stone) is *He Shouwu* (Fleeceflower Root)
- *Bilian* (Emerald Lotus) is *Mutong* (Akebia)
- *Hong Mudan* (Red Peony) is *Chishaoyao* (Red Peony Root)
- *Yangchun Mulà* (Spring Wood Wax) is *Changpu* (Acorus)
- *Kuawei Xianghui* (Appetite-Enhancing Fennel) is superior *Huixiang* (Fennel)
- *Changshengcao* (Longevity Herb) is *Duhuo* (Angelica) or *Rensu* (Perilla)
- *Jinya San* (Golden Crow Powder) is *Cao Wu* (Wild Aconite)
- *Hugu Gao* (Tiger Bone Paste) resembles *Nanxing* (Arisaema)
- *Huaishang Ju* (Huai Tangerine) is *Zhiqiao* (Bitter Orange)
- *Longquan Xiang* (Dragon Spring Incense), stir-fried, is *Junjiang* (Military Ginger)
- *Buxuezhi* (Blood-Tonifying Resin) is *Danggui* (Chinese Angelica), wine-baked
- *Baoding Xiang* (Treasure Tripod Incense) is *Jianghuang* (Turmeric)
- *Yujian* (Jade Arrow) is *Xiangbaizhi* (Fragrant Angelica)
- *Tu Wuyào* (Earth Lindera) is *Tumusu* (Earth Perilla)
- *Jinxie Xiang* (Gold Dust Incense) is *Rougui* (Cinnamon), unexposed to fire
- *Zixia Jiao* (Purple Rosy Glue) is *Zijing* (Redbud)
- *Yusui Qiongjian* (Jade Marrow Crystal) is *Fanru Mo* (Burnt Borneol and Myrrh)
- *Tianhuafen* (Trichosanthes Root) resembles *Ruixue* (Auspicious Snow)
- *Guoshi* (National Treasure) is *Gancao* (Licorice)
- When seeking formulas, these analogies may aid identification.

**Methods for Preparing Ingredients:**
- *Baizhi* and *Rougui* must not be exposed to fire.
- *He Shouwu* must not contact iron utensils.
- *Tu Wuyào*, *Chishaoyao*, *Huixiang*, *Zipanpi*, *Wangxianxiao*, and *Junjiang* are all stir-fried.
- *Duhuo* is stir-fried after removing nodes.
- *Danggui* is wine-washed and baked.
- *Zhiqiao* is aged and roasted.
- *Gancao* is honey-fried.
- *Mutong* has its skin and nodes removed.
- *Nanxing* is roasted.
- *Huai Cao Wu* is roasted.
- *Jianghuang* and *Tianhuafen* are used raw.

**Volume Four of Immortal-Transmitted External Medicine Secret Formulas (Concluded)**

**#1 Entry:** *Ming* block-printed edition lacks this.
**#2 Five:** Below this, both the *Ming* block-printed edition and manuscript copies include the character *qian* (钱).
**#3 Jiegeng (Platycodon):** The dosages for the ten ingredients above are originally missing.
**#4 Hong (Red):** The *Ming* block-printed edition reads *hua* (花, "flower").
**#5 Zhi (Angelica):** Originally written as *men zheng* (门正); emended according to the manuscript copy.
**#6 Mu (Wood):** The *Ming* block-printed edition reads *shui yi* (水一, "one part water").
**#7 Pu (Cattail):** Below this, the *Ming* block-printed edition includes a small-character annotation: *also called Yangchunxue (Spring Snow) and Wangjianxiao (Visible Dissolution).*

Collect water from the twelfth lunar month, strain silk through it, and let it settle until clear and cold. Take twenty-three doses warm; the disease will recede, and thirst will cease.

Grind into fine powder. Take 1 *qian* per dose, mixed with a small amount of freshly drawn well water, sipped slowly.

A case of massive back abscess along the meridians, extending from the shoulder to the waist and ribs, hardened like stone and dark purple-black, was treated with old medicinal applications. By midnight, severe vomiting ensued. After three or four doses of this formula, vomiting ceased, and the abscess eventually ulcerated, discharging copious red fluid. Recovery followed in forty days.

*End of Scroll Five of Immortal-Transmitted Esoteric External Medicine Formulas*

*Compiled by Zhao Yizhen, the Original Yang Master of Junyi*

**Proven Remedies for All Types of Nail-like Sores**

When a nail-like sore (*ding*) first appears, if it does not change color, causes no pain, remains immobile when pressed, has a sunken center, and is accompanied by chills and fever, this confirms the sore. Types include *qi ding*, *water-fire ding*, *snake-eye ding*, *stone ding*, *male-female ding*, *rotten ding*, *blood ding*, *axe ding*, *red-thread*, *fish-eye*, *purple-inkstone*, and *hemp-seed ding*. Immediately apply *Circle Yellow Medicine*: take one gallbladder from a male pig slaughtered in the twelfth lunar month, add realgar, Beijing ink, and ginger juice, grind into powder, and place inside the gallbladder. Apply this medicine around the sore to prevent *walking yellow* (systemic infection).

Once the *original ding* emerges, insert a needle until pain is felt, then stop. Bleeding is harmless. Apply *Immortal Toad Extract* to draw out toxins, then insert *Yellow Medicine* into the sore and cover with *Water-Sinking Plaster* or *Divine Response Plaster*. When the yellow (pus) recedes, the sore will redden and swell. Around the swollen area, use needles to drain toxic yellow fluid.

If *walking yellow* occurs, observe where the blood vessels lead and use a fire needle to sever them, halting further spread. If the yellow toxin travels and forms a lump, this confirms the infection. Insert thirty to fifty needles into the lump to drain blood and toxins, then apply *Topical Yellow Medicine* to expel the poison.

If the yellow toxin crosses from left to right or right to left, the condition is difficult to treat and likely fatal. If no blood emerges when needling the yellow area, or if the blood is dark purple or black, the condition is also critical and fatal.

When needling to extract yellow toxin, avoid iron needles—use only gold, silver, or copper needles.

At the first sign of eruption, urgently administer *Pain-Pursuing Life-Snatching Decoction* to dissolve the swelling internally, yielding immediate results. *Flying Dragon Life-Snatching Elixir* may also be used. Later, administer *Toxin-Transforming Swelling-Resolving Supporting Decoction*. All subsequent medicines should induce profuse sweating for efficacy.

**Method for Extracting Toad Venom (*Chan Su*)**
Take a live toad with scabies, press its brow ridge onto oil paper or fresh mulberry leaves, and collect the secreted *chan su* with a bamboo strip. Scrape it onto the strip and store in a shaded place overnight until dry, then collect for use.

**Toxin-Pursuing Elixir**
Removes the head of the *ding*, draws out pus and toxins, and yields immediate results.
- *Chan su* (dried), 1 *qian* (dissolve in aged wine)
- Croton seeds, 7 pieces (remove shells, retain oil)
- Sparrow droppings (*baidingxiang*), 1 *qian* (if unavailable, add croton seeds)
- Centipede (wine-soaked, baked until yellow)
- Sal ammoniac, 1 *qian*
- Realgar, 2 *qian*
- Calomel, 1 *qian*
- Cinnabar, 2 *qian* (for coating; yellow lead may substitute)

Grind all into fine powder, mix with wheat flour and water to form pills. If pills do not hold, add wine and flour paste. Shape into wheat-sized pills with pointed ends. Insert into the needle-opened sore, then cover with *Water-Sinking Plaster*. Later, apply plasters and flesh-generating medicines to expel pus, blood, and toxins.

For black, sunken, or leaking sores with dead or decayed flesh that impedes healing, this medicine also expels toxins, removes necrotic tissue, and promotes new flesh growth, leading to recovery. Use one pill for small sores; increase for larger ones.

For mild cases, needles are unnecessary. Scratch the sore’s center with a fingernail, place the medicine, and cover with *Water-Sinking Plaster*. The sore will redden and swell, indicating efficacy. Removing decayed flesh is optimal. This method is divinely effective and yields immediate results.

**Water-Sinking Plaster**
Place *bletilla* powder in a dish, add water to submerge it, then cover with paper. When applying this plaster, do not use flesh-generating medicines (formulas provided earlier).

**Topical Yellow Medicine**
- Cicada slough
- Silkworm (*jiangcan*)

**Alternative Formulas**
- Loofah leaves with tendrils, scallion whites, Chinese chives
- Cocklebur roots and shoots (burned to ash), white salt, plum ash, indigo (most effective)

Grind all into fine powder, mix with vinegar, and apply around the sore, leaving the center open to drain toxins. If the medicine dries, reapply vinegar to keep it moist. If the condition does not improve, add the aforementioned *Toxin-Transforming Blood-Dispersing Toxin-Extracting Powder* for immediate results.

For *ding* sores with sunken centers or persistent pus, use *iron rust* (quantity as needed), grind into fine powder, mix with vinegar, and apply to the sore. The toxin will naturally protrude, and pus will dry immediately, yielding swift results.

**Pain-Pursuing Life-Snatching Decoction (Secret Formula, Rapid Efficacy)**
Dissolves swelling internally.
- Notopterygium (*qianghuo*)
- Pubescent angelica (*duhuo*)
- Green tangerine peel (use more)
- Saposhnikovia (*fangfeng*) (use more)
- Coptis (*huanglian*)
- Red peony (*chishaoyao*)
- Asarum (*xixin*)
- Licorice nodes (*gancaojie*)
- Cicada slough (*chantui*)
- Silkworm (*jiangcan*)
- Coptis rhizome (*jiaolian*) (equal parts)

Add human placenta (*heche*), *ze lan*, and honeysuckle. For pus, add fleeceflower root (*shouwu*), angelica (*baizhi*) (for smooth flow), *qingmuxiang*, rhubarb (*dahuang*), gardenia (*zhizi*), and morning glory seeds (*qianniu*).

For leg sores, add chaenomeles (*mugua*).

Slice the ingredients. For each dose of 5 *qian*, first take one dose and add a small amount of *ze lan* leaves, 1 *tael* each of honeysuckle and fresh ginger. Pound together, steep in good wine, and administer warm. For those who do not drink, decoct in water.

Then use 1.5 cups each of wine and water, 10 slices of fresh ginger, and decoct until warm. Administer until sweating occurs. As the condition improves, reduce the dosage, then add 2 *qian* of rhubarb and decoct warm. Finally, induce a bowel movement to expel residual toxins.

Though the ingredients may seem unremarkable, this formula is divinely effective and infallible, with repeated proven results. If other symptoms arise, adjust the treatment accordingly for rapid efficacy.

For vexation and vomiting, add 1 *qian* of licorice nodes and administer with fermented soybean water.

For hiccups and nausea, administer with a decoction of *ru xiang*, fermented soybean powder, and licorice. Alternatively, swallow *zi he che* and aged ginger with a mouthful of rice vinegar.

For vexation and vomiting due to latent summerheat, use *Zhu Sha Wu Ling San*. For persistent hiccups, add *mu ding xiang* and *shi lian*; if unrelieved, use *Bu Huan Jin Zheng Qi San* with *ren shen* and *mu xiang*. For unceasing vomiting with thirst, cold limbs, and summerheat, swallow *Huang Lian Xiang Xu San* with *Xiao Shu Wan*. For cold hands and feet, add *xuan mu gua* and *qian niu*. For vexation, add *mai men dong*, *chi shao yao*, *shan zhi zi*, and *deng cao*. For tidal fever, add *bei chai hu*, *huang qin*, *dan zhu*, and *si mao gen*. For blurred vision, add a small amount of *zhu sha*, *xiong huang*, and *she xiang*. For abdominal distension, add *yi yi ren* and *han shui shi*. For spontaneous diarrhea, add *bai mu*, *bai fu ling*, *rou dou kou*, and *ying su ke*. For unrelenting abdominal pain, add *nan mu xiang* and *ru xiang*. For wheezing and cough, add *zhi mu*, *bei mu*, and a small amount of honey. For headache, add *chuan xiong*, *bai zhi*, and *cong bai*; if unrelieved, grind *luo bo zi*, *chuan xiong*, and *cong bai* into a paste and apply to the *tai yang* points to stop the pain. For copious phlegm, pound fresh *ai* leaf tips with rice vinegar to extract the juice, then swallow to expel phlegm. For sore throat, add *shan dou gen*, *ling xiao gen*, *shan zhi zi*, *dan zhu ye*, *ai ye*, and *deng cao*. For constipation, add ginger-processed *chi shao yao*, *zhi ke*, and *da fu pi*. For urinary retention, add *chi shao yao*, *chi fu ling*, *mu tong*, *che qian*, and *deng cao*. For bloody urine, add fresh *sheng di huang* and *che qian*. For nosebleed, add *ye hong hua*, *di huang*, *ou jie*, and fresh ginger peel. For non-painful sores that do not rise, apply three cones of moxa; if still no pain, the condition is incurable. For *gu zheng*, add *si mao gen*. For pulselessness, administer *Er Shi Si Wei Liu Qi Yin*.

The secret formula *Jin Ye Su Xiao Nei Xiao* ensures rapid efficacy for a lifetime. *Fei Long Duo Ming Dan* is specifically for *ding* sores, carbuncles of the back, brain abscesses, breast abscesses, *fu gu ju*, and all headless swellings. For such malignant sores, one dose will bring a head to headless swellings, induce pain in painless ones, and immediately cure those already formed. This is the supreme treasure among remedies for critical conditions—one dose can save a life in peril, with infallible results. A closely guarded family secret, its divine speed and efficacy are unmatched. Never lightly disclosed, it remains a lifelong treasure.

**Ingredients:**
- *Chan su*: 2 *qian* (if dried, dissolve in aged wine)
- *Xue jie*: 1 *qian*
- *Ru xiang*: 2 *qian*
- *Mo yao*: 2 *qian*
- *Xiong huang*: 3 *qian*
- *Qing fen*: 0.5 *qian*
- *Dan fan*: 1 *qian*
- *She xiang*: 0.5 *qian*
- *Tong lv*: 2 *qian*
- *Han shui shi*: 1 *qian*
- *Zhu sha*: 1 *qian* (for coating)
- *Nao zi*: 0.5 *qian* (optional)
- *Hai yang*: 21 (land snails, used with shells)
- *Tian long*: 1 (soaked in *ji* water, roasted until yellow, head and feet removed)

Grind all into fine powder, then grind the *hai yang* into a paste and mix with the powder to form pills the size of mung beans. If the pills do not hold, add wine and wheat flour paste to bind. For each dose, take two pills. First, have the patient chew a 3-inch piece of *cong bai* until pulpy, then spit it into their palm (left for men, right for women). Wrap the pills in the *cong bai* pulp and swallow with 3–4 cups of unadulterated hot wine. Have the patient rest in a wind-free place, covered with clothing. After the time it takes to walk five *li*, administer several more cups of hot wine to reinforce the medicine’s power. The treatment is complete when profuse sweating occurs. For mild cases, two pills will suffice; if no sweat appears, take two more. For severe cases lasting three to five days, take two more pills to cure. If a *ding* sore has spread to the heart (*zou huang*), it is difficult to treat; if cold sweat appears, the patient will die. If the patient cannot chew *cong bai*, grind it and wrap the pills inside, then swallow with hot wine. For sores on the upper body, take after meals; for sores on the lower body, take before meals.

After taking this medicine, avoid cold water, cucumbers, eggplants, oil, pork offal, fish, meat, damp noodles, and all foods that provoke wind or sores. Strictly avoid contact with women who have washed or changed clothes worn by those with *hu chou*. This remedy is infallible and revives the dying.

**Verse:**
*Xue jie*, *chan su*, *qing fen*,
*Xiong huang*, *tong lv*, *zhu sha* blend,
*Dan fan*, *han shui*, *she xiang* add,
*San qi*, *hai yang* ground to mend.
For *ding* sores and malignant poisons,
Brain abscesses, carbuncles—none it spares.
Two pills the size of mung beans take,
Chewed with *cong bai*, wine to partake.
Cover with clothes till sweat does flow,
A soul snatched from death—its fame aglow.
A divine elixir passed to mortal hands,
*Duo Ming Jin Dan*—priceless, grand.

For *ding* sores, abscesses, and toxic swellings, this formula ensures internal resolution, transforming toxins into black fluid expelled through urine, with infallible results. Guard this formula well—do not disclose it lightly.

**Ingredients for decoction:**
- *Ru xiang*: 1 *qian*
- *Zhi mu*: 1 *qian*
- *Ban xia*: 1 *qian*

- *Zao jiao*: 1 *qian*

Decoct the above (totaling 8 *qian*) in one bowl of unadulterated wine until half remains. Strain and administer warm in a single dose. Do not alter the proportions. Pound the dregs into fine powder, add 1 *liang* of autumn *fu rong* leaves, and mix with honey and well water to form a paste. Apply to the sore; if it dries, remoisten with honey water. By the next day, the swelling will resolve—no second dose is needed.

*Xiong Huang Wan* unblocks the bowels and eliminates toxic accumulations.

**Wei Jin Pills**

To expel phlegm, decoct *sang bai pi* (mulberry root bark) and *xing ren* (apricot kernel) and swallow the pills with the cold decoction—this will induce purgation.

Grind into fine powder, form into pills using wheat-flour paste. For each dose, take seven or nine pills with plain water. Avoid hot liquids or foods; afterward, consume warm rice porridge or rice water to restore strength.

**Ba Huang Plaster**
Use *chan su* (toad venom) and finely sifted wheat flour to form pills the size of *wutong* seeds. Place one pill beneath the tongue—yellow discharge will immediately appear.

**Bai Er San (Hundred-Two Powder), also called Heart-Protecting Powder**
For treating furuncles with restlessness, loss of limb control, or manic episodes—administer urgently.
- *Gan cao jie* (licorice root nodes)
- *Lü dou fen* (mung bean powder)
- *Zhu sha* (cinnabar): equal portions

Grind into fine powder. In severe cases, mix with water and administer.

- *She xiang* (musk): a small amount
- *Xiong huang*: 2 *qian*
- *Chan su*: 1 *zi*
- *Jiang zi*: 7 grains (husks removed, charred over a lamp flame to preserve properties)

Grind into powder, blend with *su* (toad venom paste), and apply to the tongue three times. Hold in the mouth and swallow gradually—the furuncle will rupture. **Avoid using iron utensils.**

**Proven Remedies:** (The Ming printed edition reads "Methods.")
*Xian Chuan Wai Ke Mi Fang*, Scroll Seven
Compiled by Zhao Yizhen of Junyi, the Original Yang Master

For the treatment of scrofula (*luǒlì*), regardless of its chronicity, first apply a constrictive bandage to encircle the sore. Then, use moxa fire to cauterize from the lower part upward—once a blister forms, proceed sequentially until reaching the "mother" lesion, where the treatment ceases. For each blister, place a slice of garlic and apply five to seven cones of moxa. After cauterizing each lesion, immediately apply a medicated plaster, changing it daily for immediate efficacy.

**Secret-Transmitted Medicated Plaster**
- *Zhen lǜ fěn* (true chlorite schist): 2.5 *liǎng*, stir-fried in a copper pan until yellow and desiccated
- *Rǔ xiāng* (frankincense): 0.5 *liǎng*
- *Tán xiāng* (sandalwood): 0.5 *liǎng*, baked dry
- *Xiāng jié* (or *xiāng jié*, dragon’s blood resin): 0.5 *liǎng*
- *Mò yào* (myrrh): 0.5 *liǎng* (used for pain)
- *Dǎn fán* (true chalcanthite): 0.5 *liǎng* (discontinue after toxin extraction and tissue regeneration)
- *Qīng fěn* (calomel): a small amount
- *Nán shé dǎn* (cobra gall, optional)
- *Shè xiāng* (musk, usable if broken): omit initially

Grind each into fine powder. For every 0.5 *liǎng* of the above ingredients, use 5 *liǎng* of mung bean powder, mixing with rice vinegar to form a paste. Spread on oil paper and apply. If tissue fails to regenerate, add tissue-regenerating agents to achieve healing.

**Five Fragrances Forsythia Powder**
Treats all accumulations of heat, scrofula with nodules, abscesses, malignant sores, and boils.
- *Chén xiāng* (aloeswood)
- *Lián qiào* (forsythia, stems removed)
- *Rǔ xiāng* (frankincense)
- *Shēng má* (black cohosh)
- *Dà huáng* (rhubarb, steamed for purgation or used raw)
- *Sāng jì shēng* (mistletoe, or substitute *shēng má*)
- *Dīng xiāng* (clove, twigs removed)
- *Shè gān* (belamcanda)
- *Dú huó* (angelica)
- *Mù tōng* (akebia)
- *Qiāng huó* (notopterygium)
- *Gān cǎo* (licorice)
- *Shè xiāng* (musk, usable if broken)
- *Qīng mù xiāng* (costusroot), equal portions

Chop the ingredients. For each dose, use 4 *qián* with 2 cups of water, decocting to 80% volume. Take warm after meals until malignant toxins are purged. An alternate formula adds raw *huáng qí* (astragalus).

This decoction may include bamboo sap and *máng xiāo* (mirabilite), adjusting quantities based on heat severity for optimal efficacy. Use the dregs to prepare a wash for immediate healing of sores.

**Instant-Efficacy Powder for Scrofula** (also called Six-One Powder or Beneficial Origin Powder)
For early-stage scrofula, this remedy brings immediate relief.
- *Huá shí* (talc): 1 *liǎng*
- *Gān cǎo* (licorice): 2 *qián*

First, take 1.5 *qián* of the powder per dose, mixed with rice water. Administer one dose before sleep and another at midnight.

**Alternate Formula**
- *Jīng jiè suì* (schizonepeta spikes): 4 *qián*
- *Jiāng cán* (silkworm): 4 *qián*
- *Hēi qiān niú* (black cowherb): 4 *qián*
- *Bān máo* (blister beetles): 28, heads, legs, and wings removed, stir-fried with glutinous rice until golden (discard beetles, retain rice)

Grind all into fine powder. Take 1 *qián* per dose at dawn with warm wine, expelling malignant toxins via urine. If no toxins appear, take another dose the next day; if still ineffective, on the third day at dawn, first consume thin glutinous rice porridge, then take another dose, followed by 1 *qián* of *hǔ pò* (amber) powder mixed with rush pith decoction. Success is marked by the expulsion of toxins in urine, ensuring no recurrence. For unrelenting abdominal pain or excessive toxin discharge, consume cold tea. If pain persists, grind *fáng fēng* (saposhnikovia) in water to relieve it. Do not overuse the preceding remedies.

**Arctium Pill**
Treats wind-toxin nodules, persistent scrofula, and unrelenting swelling and pain.
- *Niú bàng zǐ* (arctium): 1 *liǎng*, lightly stir-fried
- *Hé shǒu wū* (fleeceflower root): 1 *liǎng*
- *Gān bò hé* (dried mint): 2 *liǎng*
- *Xióng huáng* (realgar): 2 *liǎng*
- *Niú huáng* (cow bezoar): 2 *qián*
- *Shè xiāng* (musk): 2 *qián*
- *Zào jiǎo* (honey locust bark): 7 pieces, pounded in 2 *shēng* of water, decocted into a paste

Grind the above into powder, mixing with the honey locust paste to form pills the size of *wú tóng* seeds. Take 20 pills per dose with an astragalus decoction.

**Four Sages Powder**
For post-purgation restoration in scrofula treatment.
- *Hǎi zǎo* (sargassum)
- *Shí jué míng* (abalone shell)
- *Qiāng huó* (notopterygium)
- *Qú mài suì* (dianthus spikes), equal portions

Grind all into fine powder. Take 2 *qián* per dose with rice water.

**Proven Remedies for Throat Conditions**
The throat serves as the body’s vital passage, connecting to the stomach and facilitating breath. If heat toxins accumulate in the chest and diaphragm, generating wind-phlegm that stagnates and fails to disperse, it manifests as throat diseases—sores within the throat, resembling rotting flesh, causing swelling, pain, obstruction, and difficulty swallowing. Severe cases may produce a "double tongue." Treatment should first dispel wind-phlegm to unblock the throat, then resolve heat toxins; delay risks irrecoverable consequences. Heat toxins attacking the upper palate may cause sores called "suspended abscesses," while visceral cold can also impair swallowing. Carefully examine symptoms to apply appropriate treatments.

**Toxin-Transforming Supportive Powder**
Treats acute throat closure due to wind-heat attack, swollen and painful cheeks, as well as double and single *é* (tonsillitis), *jié hóu* (throat constriction), *chóng shé* (double tongue), and *mù shé* (wooden tongue), providing relief upon purgation.
- *Xuán shēn* (figwort)
- *Mù tōng* (akebia)
- *Dà huáng* (rhubarb, raw)
- *Dàn zhú yè* (bland bamboo leaf)
- *Shān zhī zǐ* (gardenia)
- *Shēng dì huáng* (raw rehmannia)
- *Dēng cǎo* (rush pith)

Chop the ingredients, decoct in water, and take warm.

**Emergency Gargle for Choking**
Mix *zào jiǎo* (honey locust) powder with water and sesame oil, or swallow *bǎi cǎo shuāng* (soot) for immediate relief.

**Mountain Bean Root Decoction**
Treats swollen, closed, and painful throat, gargled to expel phlegm. Holding the decoction in the mouth and swallowing brings immediate healing.
- *Shān dòu gēn* (mountain bean root, detoxifying)
- *Líng xiāo gēn* (trumpet creeper root, tissue-regenerating)
- *Shān zhī zǐ* (gardenia)
- *Dàn zhú yè* (bland bamboo leaf)
- *Ài yè* (mugwort)
- *Dēng cǎo* (rush pith)

Chop the ingredients. For drinkers, decoct with wine; for non-drinkers, use water. Pregnant women should avoid this remedy. Consuming five to six doses after throat sores heal prevents recurrence.

**Throat-Blowing Wind-Dispelling Powder**
Treats throat sores, swelling, and pain; *chán hóu fēng* (throat-constricting wind), single and double *é* (tonsillitis), *jié hóu* (throat constriction), acute throat wind, *fēi sī rù hóu* (ingested flying filaments), *chóng shé* (double tongue), and *mù shé* (wooden tongue).

**Gallbladder Vitriol and Duck-Beak Marvel**

- *Dǎn fán* (chalcanthite, roasted)
- *Nǎo zǐ* (borneol, "one character" measure)
- *Bì xuě* (sal ammoniac)
- *Bái jiāng cán* (white silkworm larvae, stir-fried and silk removed)
- *Kǔ dīng xiāng* (bitter melon pedicel, i.e., sweet melon base; use sparingly)
- *Dēng cǎo* (rush pith, ground with rice-paste slurry)

If the condition does not resolve, add:
- *Xióng huáng* (realgar)
- *Zhū yá zào* (pig’s tooth soap bean, skin removed and roasted yellow)
- *Yàn xiāo* (niter)
- *Lí lú* (veratrum, skin and core removed)

Grind all into a fine powder. Apply a small amount by blowing into the throat—if the sore has not yet formed, it will disperse swiftly; if already formed, it will rupture immediately, yielding instant healing. For severe cases, blow the powder into the nostrils. If phlegm is copious, urgently prepare a solution by grinding fresh mugwort leaves with rice vinegar, then hold in the mouth and rinse to expel phlegm, achieving immediate healing.

**Alternative Formula**
Take a pig’s gallbladder from the twelfth lunar month, fill it with *míng fán* (alum) powder, and hang it in the wind to dry. Remove the alum, grind it into powder, and blow it into the throat for instant efficacy. For those who suffer annual recurrences, moxibustion can prevent future outbreaks.

**For Lockjaw**
Use *jiǔ lóng chuān* (also called *jīn chāi cǎo*, "golden hairpin herb"), preferably a single-stemmed specimen. Peel the root, crush it, wrap in silk, and bind it to the end of a chopstick. Apply to the teeth five or six times to open the jaw, then apply similarly to the throat to expel phlegm and drool. Later, roast the herb, grind it into powder, and wrap it in silk with the powder to apply to the throat five or six times for healing. If symptoms recur after eating, crush fresh ginger, roast *zào jiǎo* (soap bean) until charred, and grind into powder. Wrap the ginger with salt and the powdered soap bean in silk, and apply to the throat five or six times for healing.

**For Acute Throat-Constricting Wind**
Grind fresh ginger with rice vinegar and administer as a rinse—throat sores will rupture immediately, yielding marvelous results.

**Drumstick Herb and Local Ox-Knee**
Add *wū yào* (lindera root) for enhanced efficacy. Grind the two fresh herbs together, extract the juice, and administer with vinegar. For severe cases, pour into the nostrils to induce phlegm expulsion and healing. If using dried herbs, grind into powder and decoct with rice vinegar.

**Divine Powder**
Treats acute seasonal *chán hóu fēng* (throat-constricting wind) with progressive throat obstruction, inability to swallow food or water, lockjaw, and loss of consciousness.
- *Xióng huáng* (realgar)
- *Huá lú shēng* (raw veratrum)
- *Bái fán* (alum)
- *Yá zào* (soap bean, skin removed and roasted yellow)
- Add *jié xiāo* (mantis egg-case, seven pieces)

Grind all into a fine powder. Administer a "one-character" dose by blowing into the nostrils to induce the expulsion of stubborn phlegm for healing.

**Alternative Formula**
Decoct *bái yào* (white medicine) and *shān dòu gēn* (mountain bean root) together, hold in the mouth to rinse and expel phlegm, then swallow one or two mouthfuls.

**Proven Remedy for Rabid Dog Bites**
If bitten by a rabid dog, immediately consume half a cup of pure oil to clear the heart. Urgently prepare *xióng huáng* (realgar) and stir-fried *bān máo* (blister beetle, with head, wings, and legs removed), grinding them into fine powder. Take one dose daily. Additionally, mix *xióng huáng* with wine and consume forty-nine doses over forty-nine days. Cold water with a small amount of pure oil may also be used, with the expulsion of foul toxins via urine as the benchmark. If urine does not flow, take an additional dose. After urination, if abdominal pain occurs, urgently mix *qīng diàn* (indigo) with cold water and consume to relieve symptoms; a decoction of *huáng lián* (coptis) may also be used. After taking the medicine, avoid consuming hot foods to ensure complete healing. Do not wait for the wound to heal—if it does not rupture, apply moxibustion to break it and release the toxic qi to prevent recurrence. If blood appears in the urine, the condition is incurable and will prove fatal. If the victim dies shortly after being bitten, urgently treat them. If symptoms appear within a year or a hundred days, and the victim is dead but the heart remains warm, they can still be saved. Urgently decoct one *jīn* (catty) of *huái huā* (pagoda tree flower) with two large bowls of water and wine, reducing it to one and a half bowls. Warm the decoction and use an implement to pry open the mouth, pouring it into the throat. After a short while, the victim will revive. One hour later, feed them warm, clear congee, and they will recover. If not, administer the dregs of the decoction. Avoid cold water and wind-inducing foods like mutton—strictly observe these prohibitions.

**Another Proven Formula**
For recurrent cases with no other remedy, this method is highly effective.
- *Míng xióng huáng* (clear realgar), five *qián* (mace)
- *Shè xiāng* (musk), five *qián*

Grind each into fine powder and mix evenly. Dissolve in two cups of wine and administer. If the patient refuses, pinch their nose and pour it down. After taking the medicine, the patient will inevitably sleep—do not disturb them. Allow them to sleep until they naturally revive, then administer the previous medicine again upon the expulsion of foul matter to achieve healing.

**Another Proven Remedy**
Grind *xìng rén* (apricot kernel, skin and tips removed) with *mǎ lán gēn* (horse orchid root) into a fine paste. First, wash the wound with a decoction of scallions, then apply the paste. Alternatively, use *xìng rén* alone (skin and tips removed).

**Another Formula**
Use fifty *bì má zǐ* (castor beans), remove the shells, and grind into a paste with well water. First, wash the wound with lye water, then apply the paste.

**Another Formula**
Apply *hǔ gǔ yóu* (tiger bone oil) for instant efficacy.

*Immortal-Transmitted External Medicine Secret Formulas, Scroll Seven*
*Immortal-Transmitted External Medicine Secret Formulas, Scroll Eight*
*Compiled by Zhao Yizhen of Junyi, the Original Yang Master*

**On Various Abscess and Sore Formulas**

The physician’s use of medicine is like a general’s use of troops. As the saying goes: "A thousand soldiers are easily found, but a single good general is hard to seek." Victory is decided by a skilled general; efficacy is decided by a skilled physician. Knowing whether troops will triumph or fall lies in the general’s command; knowing whether a patient will live or die lies in the physician’s diagnosis. Though an army may be vast, without a skilled general, the troops cannot prevail. Though a thousand formulas exist, without a skilled physician, the medicines cannot take effect. For a skilled general must have troops to command, just as a skilled physician must have medicines to prescribe. The general’s might lies in deploying unconventional tactics to defeat the enemy; the physician’s merit lies in using extraordinary remedies to cure illness. In warfare and medicine alike, one must first seek out skilled generals and physicians—without the right person, neither troops nor medicines can succeed. To preserve one’s health and avoid illness, one must follow the wise counsel of a skilled physician.

*The Sagely Benefit Formulas* states: The *Neijing* asks, how does one distinguish between abscesses and sores? When the five viscera are disharmonious, abscesses arise. Those with long-standing wasting-thirst disorders often develop abscesses and sores. Immoderate eating and drinking, erratic joy and anger, insufficient yin qi, and excess yang qi disrupt the harmony of the constructive and defensive energies, causing blood to stagnate and fail to circulate. When blood stagnates, defensive qi cannot flow, and heat accumulates, giving rise to abscesses and sores, which generate intense heat. In severe cases, the flesh putrefies into pus, yet the condition does not penetrate the muscles and skin to the bones and marrow, nor does it scorch the marrow or damage the five viscera. However, if the muscles, skin, bones, and marrow become scorched, extending inward to the five viscera, and the blood and qi are exhausted, the disease may erode the sinews and bones, leaving no healthy flesh. Abscesses and sores may arise internally or externally: internally, they form between the chest, abdomen, and viscera; externally, they manifest on the surface of the skin, flesh, muscles, and bones. Though these two poisons arise without fixed locations, they bear consistent names.

The root of abscesses lies in stagnant blood that fails to induce sweating, internal stasis that does not shed impurities, and pent-up qi that cannot disperse outward, accumulating into heat. The formation of abscesses and sores with pus and fluid is neither heaven-sent nor earth-born—it is the result of accumulation. To safeguard life, one must exhaust all medical means to prevent harm, guard against incipient signs, and address disorders before they take shape—this is the principle of "treating in the evening what is perceived in the morning." Yet illnesses arise in myriad forms, with varied triggers: as sores, boils, clove sores, abscesses, or deep-rooted sores. Though their initial signs may differ slightly, one must regard them with grave concern. Many dismiss them lightly, leading to countless deaths.

When abscesses and sores first appear, their symptoms are subtle, and most people do not take them seriously—they should be treated promptly. Heat emerging between the skin layers causes superficial swelling with small roots, growing no larger than one or two *cun*—this is an abscess. When heat from the great fu-organs surges outward, it erupts violently, producing glossy, soft swelling that spreads widely—this is an abscess or deep-rooted sore. Some cases are deficient, others excessive: deficient conditions require supplementation; excessive conditions require drainage. Those with excess heat are easier to cure; those with deficient cold and pathogenic heat are harder to treat. Swelling that is firm and yields thick pus is excessive; swelling that is soft and yields thin pus is deficient. Most treatments lack method and order, and in the moment of urgency, how can one discern the depth of the illness? This is why so many perish.

Treating abscesses and sores is like addressing a sudden collapse—where there is injury, there is suffering. Yet abscesses and sores demand urgent care. Those arising on the throat, tongue, face, brain, nape, shoulders, upper back, chest, diaphragm, or breasts—if located in perilous areas—are critical. They must be treated the same day they are detected, and recovery may still be possible. Without a skilled physician, or if one fails to grasp the analogy, even if a common doctor achieves a cure, it is mere luck. Most people do not regard these conditions as urgent, yet they truly require immediate treatment. Delay risks irreversible progression, and such cases often end in fatality. Treating these illnesses is like extinguishing a fire, rescuing the drowning, or pursuing fleeing bandits—delay invites disaster.

Abscesses and sores manifest in three types: swelling that is elevated and soft arises from the blood vessels; swelling that is sunken and firm arises from the bones and flesh; swelling with unchanged skin color arises from the bone marrow. Shallow sores favor thin areas; deep sores favor thick areas. In abscesses and sores, pressing firmly elicits pain if the pus is deep; light pressure causes pain if the pus is shallow. If the pressed area does not rebound, there is no pus—it is water. If it rebounds, pus is present. Swelling that is entirely soft is a blood tumor, not an abscess. Swelling that gradually enlarges without excessive heat, with intermittent pulling pain, is a qi tumor. These are caused by knotted accumulations that do not dissipate over time, eventually developing into abscesses, deep-rooted sores, or clove sores—all due to cold qi. When cold lingers and accumulates, extreme yin generates yang, transforming cold into heat, leading to ulceration. Such ulcers invariably become fistulas.

Yet illnesses have shifting symptoms, and diseases wax and wane. To comprehend them fully requires more than fleeting insight—medicine is an art of intention, adapting to the moment’s demands.

*The Sagely Benefit* states: Abscesses, deep-rooted sores, clove sores, breast abscesses, and boils exhibit the five favorable and seven adverse signs—these must not be overlooked. Restlessness with coughing, or abdominal pain with thirst, progressing to uncontrolled diarrhea, or urination resembling strangury—this is the first adverse sign. Copious pus and blood, severe swelling and inflammation, foul pus, and unrelenting pain—this is the second adverse sign. Labored breathing, shortness of breath, disorientation, and lethargy—this is the third adverse sign.

Gaze unsteady, with redness and swelling of the black of the eye, and the pupil turned upward—this is the fourth adverse sign. Stiffness of the shoulders and neck, with heaviness in the limbs—this is the fifth adverse sign. Inability to take food or drink, vomiting upon taking medicine, and loss of taste—this is the sixth adverse sign. Hoarse voice, pallid complexion, bluish-red lips and nose, with swelling of the face and limbs—this is the seventh adverse sign.

Pain subsiding on its own, with return of appetite and taste—this is the first favorable sign. Regular bowel movements and urination—this is the second favorable sign. Swelling and pus resolving spontaneously, with fresh color and no necrosis—this is the third favorable sign. Bright and clear spirit, with clear and resonant speech—this is the fourth favorable sign. Harmonious qi and body—this is the fifth favorable sign. If two of the five favorable signs appear, recovery is likely; if four of the seven adverse signs appear, danger is imminent.

Yet some diseases may originally share the seven adverse signs but present with tight skin like the favorable; others may originally share the five favorable signs but present with flaccid skin like the adverse. How could such subtleties be grasped by ordinary minds? If all five favorable signs converge, the condition is most auspicious. If all seven adverse signs manifest together, the crisis is severe.

Herein are recorded the diagnostic signs, supplemented with acupoint observations for clarity. If pain arises subtly in the region of *mó* over the viscera, it is *fèi jū* (lung flat-abscess); if slight swelling appears above, it is *fèi yōng* (lung abscess). If dull pain arises at *Jùquè*, it is *xīn jū* (heart flat-abscess); if slight swelling appears above, it is *xīn yōng* (heart abscess). If dull pain arises at *Qīmén*, it is *gān jū* (liver flat-abscess); if slight swelling appears above, it is *gān yōng* (liver abscess). If dull pain arises at *Zhāngmén*, it is *pí jū* (spleen flat-abscess); if slight swelling appears above, it is *pí yōng* (spleen abscess). If dull pain arises at *Zhōngwǎn*, it is *wèi jū* (stomach flat-abscess); if slight swelling appears above, it is *wèi yōng* (stomach abscess). If dull pain arises at *Jīngmén*, it is *shèn jū* (kidney flat-abscess); if slight swelling appears above, it is *shèn yōng* (kidney abscess). If dull pain arises at *Tiānshū*, it is *dà cháng jū* (large intestine flat-abscess); if slight swelling appears above, it is *dà cháng yōng* (large intestine abscess). If dull pain arises at *Dāntián*, it is *sān jiāo jū* (triple burner flat-abscess); if slight swelling appears above, it is *sān jiāo yōng* (triple burner abscess). If dull pain arises at *Guānyuán*, it is *xiǎo cháng jū* (small intestine flat-abscess); if slight swelling appears above, it is *xiǎo cháng yōng* (small intestine abscess). Examine the affected *mó* in the patient and, based on these signs, determine the depth of the abscess or flat-abscess. Identify which organ the disease originates from, what mineral or stone medicines the patient has previously consumed, assess the strength or weakness of their qi, and treat accordingly after careful consideration.

*The Thousand Gold Pieces* states: Whenever an abscess or flat-abscess first appears—whether a large boil, small boil, large abscess, or small abscess—observe it closely. At the slightest abnormality, act with urgency and treat it immediately. Cease all consumption of toxic substances to allow healthy flesh to regenerate swiftly, and abstain from sexual activity for three months. Guard against cold winds and overexertion until the sinews, blood, and vessels fully recover; only then may one act freely. If precautions are not strictly observed, new flesh may be easily damaged, and severe injury may lead to further ulceration. If the condition recurs, disaster will follow—be vigilant, be vigilant.

For all abscesses and flat-abscesses at onset, administer *Zhuīdīng Duómìng Tāng* promptly to resolve internally, or use *Huàdú Xiāozhǒng Tuōguǒ Sǎn*, *Mìchuán Nèituō Qiānjīn Sǎn*, or *Shíliù Wèi Liúqì Yǐn*. For those with pronounced yin patterns, use *Nèihán Sǎn*. During treatment, apply *Jiědú Shēngjī Sǎn* topically, along with *Huàdú Sǎn* and *Xuè Bá dú Sǎn*. After applying *Huàdú Shēngjī* ointment, use an iron tube with *Bádu Gāo* to draw out toxins. For all such eruptions, if the condition does not stabilize, apply the treatment in a ring around the lesion until the skin breaks, at which point it will cease. At the initial stage, this will cause dispersal. If pus is present, use this method to rupture the lesion and drain the pus. If drainage is incomplete, use a medicinally boiled suction tube to extract the pus, toxic fluids, and blood, and recovery will follow.

Swelling with a root spanning one to three *cùn* is a boil; two to five *cùn* is an abscess; five *cùn* to one *chǐ* is a flat-abscess; one to three *chǐ* is termed "whole-body." Lesions the size of a bean are called *páozi*. When a flat-abscess forms pus and drains from all nine orifices, and once the pus is fully expelled, use tweezers to remove necrotic flesh until the root of the pus is eliminated, then apply flesh-generating and pain-relieving medicine. Once the flesh is replenished, use a paper-shielded medicinal plaster.

It is further stated: When qi stagnates in the channels and collaterals, causing both blood and qi to congeal and fail to circulate, abscesses and flat-abscesses form. Though not initially attributed to heat, the subsequent development of abscesses arises from yang qi converging, where cold transforms into heat. When heat prevails, flesh putrefies into pus. This occurs when the body harbors heat, which is then constrained by cold, causing the vessels to congeal and stagnate, leading to the formation of abscesses and flat-abscesses.

Deep pressure eliciting pain indicates a deep-seated condition; light pressure eliciting pain indicates a shallow condition. If the pressed area does not rebound, there is no pus; if it rebounds, pus is present. If it does not rebound, the condition may still resolve. If pressing causes overall tension, pus has not yet formed. If the area is partially soft upon pressing, pus is present. If the area feels cool to the touch, there is no pus; if it is markedly hot, pus is present. Upon detecting pus, it should be drained immediately. If there is only qi-induced swelling without pus but with blood, exercise extreme caution—do not lance it. Instead, apply *Qiānjīn Bádu Gāo* to contain it.

If the periphery is firm but the center is soft, pus is certainly present; if one side is soft, pus may also be present. If the entire area is firm, it is a malignant nodule, possibly due to qi stagnation. If the entire area is soft, it is a blood mass—a blood tumor. Carefully assess firmness, softness, deficiency, and excess, as these are critical.

For long-standing firm flat-abscesses that later develop heat and localized softening, the softened area must not be lanced. For soft flat-abscesses, keep the patient warmly clothed and covered—if lanced or needled, they cannot be cured. For abscesses with pus, do not hesitate due to thick skin; drain them promptly. In cases of bone abscesses, where the skin color remains unchanged, administer *Huàdú Xiāozhǒng Tuōguǒ Sǎn* and *Zǒumǎ Sǎn* urgently, and apply *Huàdú Bádu Sǎn* topically to draw out toxins and remove stagnant blood. If no pus forms, the condition will resolve.

When blood or pus is discharged, use a needle on the skin, inserting it three to four *fen* deep. If pus is to be drained, do not pierce directly at the apex; instead, incise from the lower edge to ensure proper drainage. Once the pus is fully expelled, new flesh will grow. Apply a flesh-generating topical powder, followed by a medicated plaster, and healing will follow.

It is further stated: When an abscess arises on the neck, it is called *yāo jū* ("heavenly carbuncle"). Its appearance is large, red, and black. If not treated promptly, the heat-qi will descend into the *yuān yè* region, damaging the *Rèn mài* (Conception Vessel), and internally scorching the liver and lungs, leading to death within ten days. As the classics say: "The front of the neck is called the *xiàng*."

According to the *Wèijì Bǎoshū* ("Treasured Book of Guarding and Saving"): One *cùn* and three *fēn* behind the ear lies the "gate of life"; an abscess here is fatal, and thus *ruì dú* ("sharp toxin") is incurable. *Ruì dú* refers to the virulence of the toxin, also called *fā yí* ("swollen jaw"). When heat-qi steams upward, affecting the forehead and mouth, it may pierce the throat, causing instant death.

When a *nǎo jū* ("brain carbuncle") develops, heat-qi attacks the brain, emerging through the skin as a head lesion. Initially, it resembles a millet grain, with redness, swelling, and hardness spreading to the ears and neck, accompanied by chills, fever, and pain. If not treated urgently, the toxin will penetrate the flesh and blood, causing putrefaction and the discharge of pus and fluid from the head. As the blood and qi are internally depleted, death is inevitable.

The *Wàitái* ("Outer Platform") states: Any abscess arising in the throat is called *měng jū* ("fierce carbuncle"). If not treated promptly, blood will turn to pus, and if not drained, it will obstruct the throat, leading to death. If blood transforms into pus within half a day and the pus is not expelled, the patient will be unable to eat and will perish in three days. Another text says: Without food, *yáng* qi surges violently, consuming the brain and scorching the carbuncle, and the complexion will be joyless. Alternatively, if there is neck pain as if pierced by a needle, with heart vexation, death is certain, and it cannot be treated. One must also assess the deficiency or excess of qi and deliberate carefully in treatment.

It is further stated: When an abscess arises on the chest, it is called *jǐng jū* ("well carbuncle"). It resembles a soybean in size and will develop over three to four days. If not treated early, it will descend into the abdomen, and if it enters the abdomen without treatment, death will occur in ten days. Persistent chills and fever also lead to death.

It is also said: When an abscess arises on a toe, it is called *tuō jū* ("shedding carbuncle"). If it appears red and black without fluid, it is fatal and untreatable. If it is not red and black but contains fluid, it may be treated. If treatment fails to improve the condition, the toe must be promptly amputated to save the patient’s life; otherwise, death will follow.

The *Shènghuì* ("Sagely Benefit") states: If pus and fluid continue to discharge from a wound and the wound opening closes prematurely, though it may appear healed, the wind-toxin remains unresolved. When foul fluid recurs and discharges rapidly, it will transform into a fistula. This warning must be heeded and guarded against carefully. The formulas follow below.

First, administer *Zhuīténg Duómìng Tāng* ("Pain-Pursuing Life-Snatching Decoction") for immediate effect; the formula is provided later.

*Huàdú Xiāozhǒng Tuōguǒ Sǎn* ("Toxin-Transforming, Swelling-Reducing, Supporting and Enveloping Powder") is specifically for treating *yōng jū* (deep-rooted abscesses), *fā bèi* (back carbuncles), *fā rǔ* (breast abscesses), *gǔ yōng* (bone abscesses), *dīng chuāng* (boils), swollen toxins, and all types of malignant sores, as well as painful swollen throats.

Ingredients:
- *Rénshēn* (Ginseng; *Panax ginseng*; may be omitted if unavailable) – 6 *qián*
- *Chì fúlíng* (Red Poria; *Poria cocos*) – 6 *qián*
- *Bái mù* (White Wood; likely *Phellodendron chinense*) – 6 *qián*
- *Huáshí* (Talcum) – 2 *liǎng*
- *Jié gěng* (Platycodon; *Platycodon grandiflorus*) – 2 *liǎng*
- *Jīnyín huā* (Honeysuckle Flower; *Lonicera japonica*) – 2 *liǎng*
- *Jīngjiè suì* (Schizonepeta Spike; *Schizonepeta tenuifolia*) – 5 *qián*
- *Shān zhīzǐ* (Gardenia Fruit; *Gardenia jasminoides*) – 5 *qián*
- *Dāng guī* (Chinese Angelica Root; *Angelica sinensis*) – 1 *liǎng*
- *Chuān xiōng* (Sichuan Lovage Rhizome; *Ligusticum chuanxiong*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Huáng cáo* (likely *Huáng qín*; Scutellaria; *Scutellaria baicalensis*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Chì sháo* (Red Peony Root; *Paeonia lactiflora*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Cāng zhú* (Atractylodes Rhizome; *Atractylodes lancea*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Má huáng* (Ephedra; *Ephedra sinica*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Dà huáng* (Rhubarb; *Rheum palmatum*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Huáng qín* (Scutellaria; *Scutellaria baicalensis*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Fáng fēng* (Saposhnikovia Root; *Saposhnikovia divaricata*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Gān cǎo* (Licorice Root; *Glycyrrhiza uralensis*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Bò hé* (Peppermint; *Mentha haplocalyx*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Lián qiào* (Forsythia Fruit; *Forsythia suspensa*) – [dosage omitted]
- *Shí gāo* (Gypsum) – [dosage omitted]
- *Máng xiāo* (Mirabilite; *Natrii Sulfas*) – #6 (add *suō shā* [Amomum Fruit; *Amomum villosum*] if not using this)

Alternatively, add:
- *Guā lóu* (Trichosanthes Fruit; *Trichosanthes kirilowii*)
- *Mǔ lì* (Oyster Shell; *Ostrea gigas*)
- *Bèi mǔ* (Fritillaria Bulb; *Fritillaria thunbergii*)
- *Mù xiāng* (Costus Root; *Saussurea costus*)

For *dīng chuāng* (boils), add *jiǎo lián* (likely *Bletilla striata*) and *hé chē* (likely *Lotus Node; Nelumbo nucifera*).

For *chén gān* (infantile malnutrition), add *chē qián zǐ* (Plantain Seed; *Plantago asiatica*), *mù tōng* (Akebia Stem; *Akebia quinata*), and *zhú yè* (Bamboo Leaf; *Phyllostachys nigra*). For pain, add *rǔ xiāng* (Frankincense; *Boswellia carteri*) and *mò yào* (Myrrh; *Commiphora myrrha*). For sore throat, add *dà huáng* (Rhubarb Root; *Rheum palmatum*), *zhī zǐ* (Gardenia Fruit; *Gardenia jasminoides*), *zhú yè* (Bamboo Leaf), and *dēng cǎo* (Rush Pith; *Juncus effusus*). For *jiǎo qì* (beriberi), add *xuān mù guā* (Chaenomeles Fruit; *Chaenomeles speciosa*) and *bīng láng* (Areca Seed; *Areca catechu*). For cough, add *bàn xià* (Pinellia Rhizome; *Pinellia ternata*) prepared with ginger juice, and decoct with fresh ginger.

Grind the above ingredients, take five *qián* per dose, decoct with one bowl of water and one scallion stalk until hot, and consume until sweating occurs. If bowel movements occur three to five times after consumption, the effect is optimal. Severe illnesses will resolve within three to five doses, with internal toxins fully dissolved.

**Internal-Supporting Thousand-Gold Powder** specializes in treating *yōng jū* (deep-rooted abscesses), *fā bèi* (back carbuncles), *nǎo jū* (scalp abscesses), *rǔ yōng* (breast abscesses), and various malignant *jié lòu* (festering sores).

*Rén shēn* (Ginseng; *Panax ginseng*)
*Dāng guī* (Chinese Angelica Root; *Angelica sinensis*)
*Huáng qí* (Astragalus Root; *Astragalus membranaceus*)
*Bái sháo yào* (White Peony Root; *Paeonia lactiflora*)

*Fáng fēng* (Saposhnikovia Root; *Saposhnikovia divaricata*)
*Guān guì* (Cinnamon Bark; *Cinnamomum cassia*)
*Jié gěng* (Platycodon Root; *Platycodon grandiflorus*)
*Bái zhǐ* (Angelica Root; *Angelica dahurica*)
*Gān cǎo* (Licorice Root; *Glycyrrhiza uralensis*)
*Guā lóu* (Trichosanthes Fruit; *Trichosanthes kirilowii*)
*Jīn yín huā* (Honeysuckle Flower; *Lonicera japonica*) – equal parts each

For severe pain, add *dāng guī*, *sháo yào*, *rǔ xiāng*, and *mò yào*.

Grind the above ingredients, take seven to eight *qián* per dose, decoct with two large cups of water until reduced to seven parts, add half a cup of good wine, strain, and consume warm. Take two to three doses daily. If black blood or sweat appears at the wound site, this indicates the efficacy of the medicine. Regardless of the severity of the condition, use one *liǎng* of the medicine, decoct with one large bowl of water, and consume with wine. If the abscess has not yet formed, it will disperse; if already formed, it will rupture.

**Secret Sixteen-Ingredient Qi-Flowing Decoction** (also called **Internal-Nourishing Powder**): The first ten ingredients are the same as the **Internal-Supporting Powder**.

*Rén shēn* (Ginseng; *Panax ginseng*) – remove the rhizome
*Dāng guī* (Chinese Angelica Root; *Angelica sinensis*) – wine-washed
*Guān guì* (Cinnamon Bark; *Cinnamomum cassia*) – remove the bark
*Chuān xiōng* (Sichuan Lovage Rhizome; *Ligust

First fumigate, then wait until the temperature cools before use. Wash the area thoroughly, then pat dry with silk cloth. Apply the **Flesh-Generating Pain-Relieving Powder**, mixed with clear oil to a stiff consistency, spreading it evenly. If the wound is wet, apply dry powder. Do not leave an opening if there is no pus; change the dressing once daily. If pus is present, leave an opening to drain toxins and remove pus. After applying the medicine, cover with black paper and bind tightly with silk strips, wrapping three to five turns. Change the outer dressing every three days, avoiding disturbance, and efficacy will soon manifest. If necrotic flesh must be removed: take one live centipede, place it in a bamboo tube filled with oil to drown, then calcine and grind into powder. Add a small amount to the previous application and apply for one full day. If pain persists, wash off this medicine and reapply the previous one; the pain will cease, and new flesh will grow.

Apply the medicine around the wound. The **Toxin-Resolving Blood-Dispersing Toxin-Extracting Powder** treats all malignant sores and foot ailments:

Grind all into fine powder and apply with fresh ginger (including the dregs) and vinegar. For rapid dispersal, add crushed garlic and apply; the toxic qi will be expelled with immediate effect.

For constipation of the viscera, take orally:

The above formula specializes in treating cold-damp *jiǎo qì* (leg qi), *fā bèi* (carbuncles of the back), all malignant sores, boils, and various toxic swellings, dispersing them upon application. It also powerfully relieves pain. Taken internally, it warms and harmonizes, resolves toxins, and disperses blood stasis. For internal support, take a large piece of fresh ginger (with skin), pound it with the medicine in high-quality ash-free wine, and consume warm. For ailments in the upper body, take after meals; for those in the lower body, take before sleep. After one or two doses, the swelling will fully resolve.

**Iron Tube Toxin-Extracting Plaster** punctures and resolves all sores and boils; if applied at the initial stage, the swelling will disperse immediately:
*Shí huī* (High-quality lime) – uncalcined
*Zào jiǎo* (Chinese Honeylocust; *Gleditsia sinensis*) – fumigated
*Nuò mǐ* (Glutinous Rice; *Oryza sativa* var. *glutinosa*)
*Nán xīng* (Jack-in-the-Pulpit Rhizome; *Arisaema erubescens*)

*Náo shā* (Sal Ammoniac; *Ammonium chloride*) – add only to remove *dīng* (deep-rooted boils), omit otherwise
*Shā gǔ niú* (Cow Bezoar)
*Bān máo* (Mylabris; *Mylabris phalerata*)
Boil the above with the following ashes in hot water, strain the liquid, and mix with the previous medicine for use:
*Zhēn tàn huī* (Charcoal Ash)
*Sāng chái huī* (Mulberry Wood Ash)
*Zhī ma huī* (Sesame Ash)
*Zào jiǎo* (Chinese Honeylocust) – 3–4 pieces
*Liǔ chái huī* (Willow Wood Ash)
Simmer the ash liquid in a pot over low heat until a white frost forms on the surface, then remove from heat. Store in a vessel and use to mix with the previous medicine. Apply sparingly three to five times; once the skin breaks and toxic qi is expelled, stop when fluid ceases.

**Toxin-Sucking Bamboo Tube** for *fā bèi* (carbuncles of the back), *yōng jū* (deep-rooted abscesses), *dīng chuāng* (deep-rooted boils), and toxic swellings—use this to draw out pus, blood, and foul fluid:
*Cāng zhú* (Atractylodes Rhizome; *Atractylodes lancea*) – equal parts
*Bái liǎn* (Ampelopsis Root; *Ampelopsis japonica*)
*Wū jiù pí* (Chinese Tallow Tree Bark; *Triadica sebifera*)
*Hòu pò* (Magnolia Bark; *Magnolia officinalis*)
*Ài yè* (Mugwort Leaf; *Artemisia argyi*)
*Hǎo chá yá* (Fine Tea Buds)
*Bái jí* (Bletilla Rhizome; *Bletilla striata*)
*Bái jí lí* (Tribulus Fruit; *Tribulus terrestris*)
Prepare by taking three, five, or seven bamboo tubes, each one *cùn* long, leaving a node at one end. Peel the green outer layer until as thin as paper, then use according to size. Boil the tubes with the above medicine for ten rounds until the medicine is fully absorbed.

While the bamboo tubes are still hot, press them firmly onto the sore, tightly adhering to the wound. When filled with pus, blood, or fluid, they will naturally detach. If not, remove them by hand and replace with another tube. Repeat this three to five times until the toxin is fully drawn out, then apply flesh-generating medicine. Once the wound is filled internally, use a medicated plaster to achieve healing.

The *Immortal Formula Paper-Isolated Plaster* (also called *Divine Response Plaster*) is specially effective for treating *fā bèi* (deep-rooted back abscesses), *rǔ yōng* (breast abscesses), *wài lián* (lower leg ulcers), and all kinds of malignant toxic sores and boils.

*Huáng lián* (Coptis Rhizome) – half *liǎng*
*Hé shǒu wū* (Fleeceflower Root, peeled) – half *liǎng*
*Cǎo wū* (Wild Aconite, peeled) – half *liǎng*
*Dāng guī wěi* (Chinese Angelica Tail) – half *liǎng*
*Bái zhǐ* (Dahurian Angelica Root) – half *liǎng*
*Chuān wū* (Sichuan Aconite, peeled) – two and a half *qián*

Later, add:
*Huáng dān* (Lead Oxide) – one *liǎng* (two *liǎng* in summer)
*Rǔ xiāng* (Frankincense) – half *liǎng*
*Mò yào* (Myrrh) – half *liǎng*
*Xuè xuě* (Dragon’s Blood) – half *liǎng*

Grind all the above ingredients coarsely. Heat five *liǎng* of clear oil with the medicine in a pot over a gentle flame until the herbs turn black. Strain through cloth to remove the dregs, then return the medicated oil to the pot. Add *huáng dān* and stir continuously with a bundle of peach and willow twigs. When the mixture turns black again, add the finely ground *xuè xuě*, *rǔ xiāng*, and *mò yào*, stirring well. Briefly heat until the mixture forms beads in water that do not disperse. Transfer to an earthenware bowl and soak in cold water for a full day and night to remove the fire toxin. The result is divinely effective.

The *Great Unity Divine Response Plaster* (also called *Golden Thread Universal Plaster* or *Ten-Thousand Spirits Plaster*) is specially effective for treating *fā bèi* (deep-rooted back abscesses), *yōng jū* (deep-rooted boils), *zhàng chuāng* (flogging wounds), malignant sores, traumatic injuries, heart pain, leg qi, and lumbar pain—none it does not cure.

*Chuān wū* (Sichuan Aconite) – one *qián*
*Cǎo wū* (Wild Aconite) – half *qián*
*Huáng lián* (Coptis Rhizome) – two *qián*
*Huáng bǎi* (Phellodendron Bark) – one *qián*
*Chì sháo yào* (Red Peony Root) – one *qián*
*Bái sháo yào* (White Peony Root) – one *qián*
*Xuán hú suǒ* (Corydalis Rhizome) – one *qián*
*Guī wěi* (Chinese Angelica Tail) – one and a half *qián*
*Liáng jiāng* (Galangal Rhizome) – half *qián*
*Mù biē* (Momordica Seed, shelled) – half *qián*
*Jiāng cán* (Silkworm, silk removed) – one *qián*
*Luàn fà* (Human Hair, the size of a chicken egg, burned to ash, added later) – one *qián*
*Zǐ kè pí* (Bark of *Citrus wilsonii*) – half *qián*
*Dì lóng* (Earthworm) – half *qián*
*Shí nán téng* (Pieris Vine) – half *qián*
*Chuān shān jiǎ* (Pangolin Scales) – half *qián*
*Bái zhǐ* (Dahurian Angelica Root) – half *qián*
*Chuān xiōng* (Sichuan Lovage Rhizome) – half *qián*
*Qiān niú* (Morning Glory Seed) – half *qián*
*Huái huā* (Pagoda Tree Flower) – half *qián*
*Wǔ bèi zǐ* (Chinese Gall) – half *qián*
*Dì gǔ pí* (Wolfberry Root Bark) – half *qián*
*Xìng rén* (Apricot Kernel) – half *qián*
*Huā jiāo* (Sichuan Pepper) – half *qián*
*Huí xiāng* (Fennel Seed) – half *qián*
*Máo xiāng* (Vetiver Root) – half *qián*
*Xuán shēn* (Figwort Root) – half *qián*
*Cāng ěr cǎo* (Xanthium Herb) – half *qián*
*Guì pí* (Cinnamon Bark) – half *qián*
*Nán xīng* (Jack-in-the-Pulpit Rhizome) – half *qián*
*Guā lóu* (Trichosanthes Fruit) – half *qián*
*Kǔ shēn* (Sophora Root) – half *qián*
*Cāng zhú* (Atractylodes Rhizome) – half *qián*
*Wǔ jiā pí* (Acanthopanax Root Bark) – half *qián*
*Fáng fēng* (Saposhnikovia Root) – one *qián*
*Shú dì huáng* (Prepared Rehmannia Root) – one *qián*
*Mì tuó sēng* (Litharge) – one *qián*
*Dīng xiāng* (Clove) – one *qián*
*Nèi xiāo* (Sal Ammoniac) – one *qián*
*Yè běn* (Wild Angelica Root) – one *qián*
*Shēng dì huáng* (Fresh Rehmannia Root) – two and a half *qián*
* Hé shǒu wū* (Fleeceflower Root) – one and a half *qián*
*Xì xīn* (Asarum Herb) – one and a half *qián*
*Jiāng zǐ* (Croton Seed, shelled) – twenty-five seeds
*Rén shēn* (Ginseng Root) – two and a half *qián*
*Bì má zǐ* (Castor Bean, shelled) – twenty-five seeds
*Hàn lián cǎo* (Eclipta Herb) – half *qián*
*Huáng qí* (Astragalus Root) – one *qián*
*Bǎi yào jiān* (Sumac Gallnut) – two and a half *qián*
*Qiāng huó* (Notopterygium Root) – one *qián*
*Nán mù xiāng* (Costus Root) – half *qián*
*Gān cǎo jié* (Licorice Node) – one *qián*
*Dì yú gēng* (Burnet Root) – one *qián*
*Dà yān zhī* (Rouge) – one *qián*

Grind the above ingredients coarsely. Soak in one *jīn* and four *liǎng* of clear oil for one or two nights, then heat in a pot over a gentle flame until the herbs turn black. Strain through cloth to remove the dregs, then return the medicated oil to the pot over a gentle flame. Gradually add the following finely ground powders:

*Nán mù xiāng* (Costus Root) – two and a half *qián*
*Ān xī xiāng* (Benzoin) – two *qián*
*Hǔ pò* (Amber) – two and a half *qián*
*Yún xiāng* (Clove) – one *qián*
*Rǔ xiāng* (Frankincense) – half *liǎng*
*Mò yào* (Myrrh) – half *liǎng*
*Xuè xuě* (Dragon’s Blood) – half *liǎng*
*Xiāng jié* (Resin of *Dalbergia odorifera*, or *Jiàng zhēn xiāng* knots) – half *liǎng*
*Sháo fěn* (Lead Carbonate) – one *qián*
*Zì rán tóng* (Pyrite, vinegar-quenched) – one and a half *qián*
*Sāng bái pí* (Mulberry Root Bark) – five *qián*
*Bái jí* (Bletilla Rhizome) – five *qián*
*Bái liǎn* (Ampelopsis Root) – five *qián*
*Xióng huáng* (Realgar) – five *qián* (dried over fire)
*Huáng dān* (Lead Oxide) – six *liǎng* (add two *liǎng* in summer)

First, add the medicated oil, then the *huáng dān*, stirring continuously with peach, willow, or pagoda tree twigs.
Next, add *zì rán tóng*, *bái jí*, *sāng bái pí*, *bái liǎn*, and *sháo fěn*.
Then add *mù xiāng*, *hǔ pò*, *ān xī xiāng*, *yún xiāng*, *rǔ xiāng*, *xuè xuě*, *mò yào*, and *xiāng jié*. When the mixture turns black and forms beads in water that do not disperse, pour it into an earthenware bowl. Soak in water for two or three days to remove the fire toxin, then leave it on the ground for another three to five days for best results. Apply as needed.

To make the *Golden Thread Plaster*, grind all the above medicines into fine powder. Melt one *jīn* of clear *sōng xiāng* (pine resin) in a pot, strain through palm fiber, then add four *liǎng* of clear oil and heat until cooked. Add one *liǎng* of *huáng dān* and continue heating until the mixture forms beads in water that do not disperse. Remove and soak in water, changing the water daily (every other day in winter).

For *fā bèi* (deep-rooted back abscesses) that appear black and painless, this indicates a *yīn* condition. Boil one *jīn* of *ài yè* (mugwort leaf) with half a *liǎng* each of *xióng huáng* (realgar) and *liú huáng* (sulfur) for half a day. Apply warm, then boil fresh mugwort and reapply ten or more times. If pain occurs, recovery is possible; if no pain and black blood appears, death is certain.

For *fā bèi* that spreads uncontrollably at onset, use this medicine to encircle the sore:
Burn *bái yán méi* (salted plum) and *zào jiǎo* (Chinese honey locust) to ash, retaining their nature, and grind into fine powder. For cases without heat, mix with rice vinegar and apply around the sore to contain it; replace frequently to prevent spreading. Adding ginger juice to the vinegar enhances the effect. For cases with heat, mix with tea infusion and apply to encircle the sore—immediate efficacy.

This treatment is most divinely effective for *fā bèi* whether already ulcerated or not.

Magnolia bark (processed with ginger) 2 *qián*; tangerine peel (white pith removed); roasted licorice node 2 *qián*; *cāng zhú* (soaked in rice-water wash) 5 *qián* in weight. Add *sāng huáng gū* 5 *qián* and grind all together into fine powder. For ulcerated sores, apply dry; for unbroken sores, mix with clear oil and apply.

*Internal Stabilizing Heart-Clearing Powder*: Treats virulent sores with intense heat, swelling, pain, thirst, and restlessness. This medicine resolves toxins with divine efficacy.
Cinnabar; *fú líng*; ginseng; white cardamom; realgar; mung beans; *pò xiāo*; licorice; borneol; musk; Chinese honey locust—equal parts.
Grind all into fine powder. Take 1 *qián* per dose, mixed with honey water.

*Pus-Penetrating Powder*: Treats all kinds of abscesses and bone-adhering sores that do not rupture. Without need for needles or blades, a single dose will cause spontaneous penetration within hours. Repeated use proves effective.
Silkworm cocoons from which moths have emerged.
Burn the cocoons to ash, mix with wine, and administer. The sore will immediately penetrate.
**Caution**: Do not use two or three cocoons when burning. If one cocoon is used, only one sore opening will form; if two or three are used, multiple openings will appear. Do not take this lightly.

*Compendium of Divine Transmissions in External Medicine, Scroll Eight* (conclusion)

**1** *Treatises on Various Abscesses and Sores*: This heading was originally absent and has been added based on the text’s content.
**2** *hé*: Likely should read *lì* (efficacy).
**3** *mó*: Appears as *mù* in *Secret Transmissions in External Medicine*.
**4** *mó*: Appears as *mù* in *Secret Transmissions in External Medicine*.
**5** *mó*: Appears as *mù* in *Secret Transmissions in External Medicine*.
**6** *máng xiāo*: Dosages for the thirteen ingredients from *chuān xiōng* upward are originally missing.
**7** *xuè xiē*: Appears as *xuè jié* (dragon’s blood) in *Secret Transmissions in External Medicine*.
**8** *le*: Originally *zǐ*; amended per manuscript.
**9** *èr liǎng*: Likely missing the character *gè* (each) above.
**10** *tí*: Likely should read *tí* (hoof).
**11** *shā*: Likely a small-script annotation, to be read with the preceding character.
**12** Dosage for *rǔ xiāng* (frankincense) originally missing.
**13** *mò*: Manuscript includes the character *cài* below.
**14** *dà*: Absent in manuscript.
**15** *dà yān zhī*: Appears as *wǔ líng zhī* in manuscript.

*Collected and Verified Divine Transmissions in External Medicine*
**Title**: *Collected and Verified Divine Transmissions in External Medicine*
**Alternate Title**: *Divine Transmissions in External Medicine: Secret Formulas*
Compiled by Yáng Qīngsǒu of the Yuán dynasty; edited by Zhào Yízhēn of the Míng dynasty.
Eleven scrolls.
**Source Text**: *Daoist Canon of the Zhèngtǒng Era*, *Tàipíng* Section.
**Collated with**: Míng dynasty block-printed edition; Qīng dynasty manuscript held at the National Library of China (hereafter "manuscript"); block-printed *Secret Transmissions in External Medicine*.

*Divine Transmissions in External Medicine: Secret Formulas, Scroll Nine*
Collected by Zhào Yízhēn, the Yuányáng Master of Jùnyí.

**On the Forms and Symptoms of Back Abscesses**

**Abscess of the Back**
An abscess arising on the back may span a foot in width and penetrate a foot in depth. If it ulcerates to the bone but does not breach the membrane, the patient will not die. This condition stems from consuming contaminated food, whereby the toxin lodges between the spleen and abdomen. Urgent treatment must target the poison in the spleen and abdomen, attacking it both internally and externally. As the spleen readily putrefies, swift administration of medicine is essential. At onset, employ *Decoction for Pursuing the Nail and Seizing Life* to achieve internal resolution (the formula appears at the end). Use *Powder for Transforming Toxin, Dispersing Swelling, and Supporting the Interior* and *Powder for Internal Support Worth a Thousand Gold* to consolidate. During the intermediate stage, apply *Medicine for Resolving Toxin, Generating Flesh, and Alleviating Pain* to the center, and *Powder for Drawing Out Toxin* around the periphery. Conclude with *Ointment for Generating Flesh* to ensure efficacy.

**Lotus-Seed Abscess**
This abscess arises on the right, where the toxin from the spleen may surge toward the heart. Given the intensity of heart fire, oral medicine must be used to disperse it, while topical applications intercept the toxin to prevent it from attacking the heart. If the entire back swells, it is incurable; only if the swelling subsides can it be treated.

All sores—whether painful or itchy—originate in the heart, for the heart governs the blood and circulates qi. For migratory painful sores, apply *Powder with Supreme Monarch* and administer fire needling three or four times at the site for optimal effect. Use the aforementioned *Powder for Transforming Toxin, Dispersing Swelling, and Supporting the Interior*, adding *arisaema*, *wild aconite*, *momordica*, *fritillaria*, *garlic*, and *fresh ginger*, mixed with rice vinegar. Apply while leaving an opening, and within two or three days, the swelling will fully resolve. Keep the area moistened with vinegar.

**Honeycomb Abscess**
This abscess, with its head at the top, is most difficult to treat and constitutes a counterflow pattern. Careful medication is required, for it is called *Honeycomb Abscess*. Treatment relies on oral medicine to support the interior, generate flesh, alleviate pain, and disperse blood, lest the toxin attack the heart and penetrate the membrane, rendering it nearly incurable due to lingering heart fire.

**Migratory Scrofula Abscess**
Commonly misattributed to the "turtle’s shadow" in folk sayings, medical texts contain no such reference. The notion that a turtle’s offspring causes this on the back is unfounded; rather, the toxin rides wind-heat to migrate. This condition arises from excessive wind generating extreme heat, which in turn causes qi to scatter. Urgent dispersal of wind and settling of heat will naturally calm the qi. The medicine for this is akin to deploying troops to quell disorder. If the toxin flows to the hands, feet, or legs, death is certain.

**Abscess of the Kidney Transport**
This condition results from dampness, pent-up anger, and consumption of hot liquor, injuring the internal kidneys and causing toxin to flow to the kidney transport points. Swift application of both oral and topical medicines is required to resolve the toxin within the kidneys, employing flesh-generating remedies in a dual internal-external assault. If the abscess arises in the yin aspect and damages the kidney membrane, it becomes difficult to treat. Strictly avoid anger and sexual activity; even minor transgressions will render it incurable. Exercise utmost caution.

**Dual Abscess of the Kidney Transport and Spleen Abscess**
This condition manifests as dual abscesses below the kidney transport points, caused by consuming hot liquor, engaging in sexual activity, harboring anger, or contracting dampness. If it arises in the yang aspect externally, it is treatable. However, if phlegm or yin abscesses damage the kidney membrane, resulting in thin pus, this indicates deficiency and is nearly incurable, inevitably fatal.

A spleen abscess arising between the left shoulder blades may be treated at onset by pricking with a lamp wick and inducing sweating with *Decoction for Pursuing the Nail*, which will disperse it.

**Right Shoulder Abscess**
This abscess arises on the right shoulder bone. Though treatable, it is difficult to stabilize due to movement. If it spreads to the left shoulder, it becomes nearly incurable. Apply medicinal powders as directed in the *Left Shoulder Abscess* formula.

**Left Shoulder Abscess**
This abscess arises on the left shoulder bone. Though treatable, it is difficult to stabilize due to movement. If it spreads to the right shoulder, it becomes nearly incurable.
